### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=dolson]
The point is, the user-friendliness/not having to use apt/synaptic bullet point that Kevin made is null, unless CNR is installed by default. Do you argue this?[/QUOTE] Yes, I do argue this.

As far as I can tell, there are generally three types of Ubuntu users:

1. Super-experts. They've used Linux for years, and they know a hell of a lot more than I do.

2. Medium folk. These people don't know much about Linux, but they have used Windows for a long time. They may be frustrated at first, installing and configuring an operating system themselves, but they ask questions on these forums, learn to edit a few configuration files, and they learn how to use their system.

3. Super-beginners. They have no clue. They just want to use their computers, not set them up. They probably just use email and a web browser. They probably **did not install and configure their own Ubuntu. Someone else set it up for them**.

CNR is for this last group (#3). They would not be apt-getting CNR because whoever set it up for them would apt-get it for them. 

Group #2 does not need CNR because they've gone through the process of possibly doing *sudo gedit /etc/apt/sources.list* and *sudo dpkg-reconfigure xserver-xorg*--using Synaptic Package Manager is a piece of cake after all that.

Group #3 likewise does not need (or want) CNR.

I think this is a new Gnome less is better decision to not keep all the menu options available by default. In my opinion it is a big mistake to not have all the options on the menu by default and Ubuntu should change the default for Dapper. This would prevent confusion for both noob's and even experienced users who are going to wonder where all their favorite apps are. Otherwise I love the new utility to clean up the menu from less used applications.

---

### Post by dolson on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=mstlyevil]I think this is a new Gnome less is better decision to not keep all the menu options available by default. In my opinion it is a big mistake to not have all the options on the menu by default and Ubuntu should change the default for Dapper. This would prevent confusion for both noob's and even experienced users who are going to wonder where all their favorite apps are. Otherwise I love the new utility to clean up the menu from less used applications.[/QUOTE]
I agree. I wouldn't call myself a noob, and I was confused at why it wasn't there. I didn't ever imagine my Linux telling me that it knows best like Windows does... And it does it sloppily too, because my menu still had the separator there.. It looked pretty silly. Chalk another one against GNOME in my books. Fits right in with the "you shouldn't want to configure a screensaver" thing.
---
I think Ubuntu is a fine distro for anyone. There's no reason to recommend another distro just because a user needs to learn to install a piece of software. Just like I choose to help improve Ubuntu for audio users instead of recommending they install Fedora and Planet CCRM, I think that software installation *in general* can be improved in Ubuntu for new users. The Add Applications dialog that mstlyevil and I have been discussing is a good start, but search for MP3 and you don't get a result for XMMS or Beep-Media-Player. It obviously needs some loving to really benefit uninitiated users.

Getting back to Peter, he went and downloaded some software that he found on his own, and then asked me how he installed it when he couldn't get it to compile. I then pointed him at Synaptic. I had to do it several times. So clearly, there must be a better way than always having to have someone else around to hold your hand. Peter did very well on his own, in my opinion, getting his system installed by himself, and getting around in the OS and getting onto the net. But, the very fact that users have to ask for help, or immediately go to the web to download things that are readily available in the repositories means that there is not enough, in fact nothing, being done to educate users on even the most basic of levels about installing software. Couple this with the apparent removal of the Add Applications menu entry by default, and you've got a pretty much hopeless case. I'd like to see it change, but my time is limited, and I choose to spend that time on audio issues. I can only hope that someone else sees the need and figures out a reasonable way to solve the problem so that everybody wins. But swerving people away from what is in my opinion the best desktop distro available is surely not the answer. Perhaps CNR is part of the solution, but it sure isn't all of it.

---

### Post by darkmatter on 2006-03-03
Ubuntu will always remain free... using the subscription services offered by CNR is and will be *OPTIONAL*...

It is still a matter of personal choice... the only question is hoe would CNRs implementation/integration be handled. I've made a post about my thoughts on the subject.

a) want to avoid potential legal issues with the likes of quicktime, w32codecs, libdvdcss2, etc

b) want quick and easy access to pay toys like Star Office and the like...

Personally, I'd use good old repos for nearly everything, though legal copies of LICENSED codecs would be nice...

### Post by DrFunkenstein on 2006-03-03
Really, this debate is getting silly.

So some company is considering offering additional software and services for ubuntu, and our selfproclaimed freedom fighters, who don't even have the slightest clue about free software, as becomes painfully obvious when reading their posts, act as if it is the end of the world and if this was somehow against the spirit of ubuntu.

Here's a newsflash, getting other vendors to offer software and services for ubuntu is exactly the way canonical (you know, the company that pays for all this stuff you are using) hopes to make money.
Case in point:
[http://www.ubuntu.com/news/db2cert](http://www.ubuntu.com/news/db2cert)

And though it seems to complicated to comprehend I can only repeat it again and again, CNR will be an optional software you can use, or, if you don't want to, refuse use, just like any other third party propietary software that is available for ubuntu.

### Post by Mariano on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Bandit]M$ and Apple are multi million dollar corporations. They didn’t get on top buy being nice all the time. I honestly believe if Linux is going to survive in the corporate world and start taking a real foot hold in the market, then it will have to have a balance of good and bad. If we try to be "good" all the time and use only open source software and never use MP3 codecs or DVD decryption software and so one we will never succeed. There is always going to have to be some proprietary software out there. Sometimes it is a necessary evil. If we are ever, going to get hardware makers to start putting out better hardware drivers (proprietary or not) then we have to get Linux into a larger more financially feasible side of the market.
[/QUOTE]

I am sorry but I do not think "balancing good and bad" is any good. If you want to be the next Apple and Microsoft, well, maybe you are right and that is needed (but all the evidence points to the fact that balancing does not much work and that one tends to pick one side rather quickly...) But I do not think the Free Software movement *wants* to become the next Microsoft and Apple. I know I do not.

"Necessary evil" is a phrase way too often heard in turning points. You guess where those turns are usually headed to, right?

### Post by darkmatter on 2006-03-03
sheesh... some of the posts in this thread are... rediculous.. I mean... I've been using pcs in general... and for a large part the unices an oss since... sheesh... when linux was just a baby... hehe... I used to use them green monochrome tin cans ;)

And the one important life lesson I learned long ago... and which many of you don't seem to realize... is exactly what 'freedom' is...

because by denying choice... or by not informing a user of those choices... you are essentially attempting to deny him/her... them... at some level... the freedom you hold so high... ### Post by Brunellus on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=darkmatter]sheesh... some of the posts in this thread are... rediculous.. I mean... I've been using pcs in general... and for a large part the unices an oss since... sheesh... when linux was just a baby... hehe... I used to use them green monochrome tin cans ;)

That may seem harsh... its not meant to be... I'm often blunt when I'm tired... this is no exception... I don't feel like being subtle...

I linked someone to these debates earlier... to get an outsiders opinion on the mindset of our community of late... and to quite honest... the response was one word... something that some of us count before bed... :-#[/QUOTE]
there is a misperception that KC wants CNR to be the default package management system for Ubuntu.  This is not--cannot!--be the case.  

So, in principle, there is really no difference between running CNR or CrossoverOffice or Cedega on your Ubuntu system.  None of them come installed by default because of licensing issues;  but you can put them there if you want.  

I think Linspire have earned themselves a lot of hostility from Linux enthusiasts because of a number of mis-representations on their part about the state of Linux on the desktop.  On the CNR site at Linspire, they make it seem like *all* software installs are ridiculously difficult--tarballs, ./configure, make, make install deals--which is simply not the case with apt (or indeed yum, yast, or portage).  

Even if that presentation rubs me, personally, the wrong way, I have no objection to CNR being offered AS AN OPTION, BY LINSPIRE for Ubuntu and the other Debian Daughters.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Brunellus]there is a misperception that KC wants CNR to be the default package management system for Ubuntu.  This is not--cannot!--be the case.[/QUOTE]

This would never be the case.  Mark wouldn't allow it.  It would be something the user would have to download and install, or apt from a Linspire server.

I stated as much several times in this thread.  This is simply an OPTION some may like, and others can choose to ignore. 

My question posed was not "is it good to have more choices" (for me, of course it is, you take or leave the choices YOU choose), but rather "is THIS a choice YOU would WANT to have, or would it be a waste of time for Linspire to bother offering this."

Even if it appears enough of you would want it, but so many others would be repulsed by the idea, we'd pass.  That's how it's shaping up, it would appear.  Many of you like the idea, but others find it offensive.  This is where some people probably avoid Ubuntu, because ironically it limits choice, because some choices are abhorrent to them.  At Linspire we let the user decide, and that is why we put any and all software that we can in the CNR Warehouse.  I don't like playing God and saying what gets in or what must be kept out, I say, "put it in" and give our users the respect of knowing they are perfectly capable of deciding for themselves.  They don't need me protecting them from certain choices.

My guess is this may be one reason Mark might actually like seeing this, as it lets him stop having to take the "God" role, deciding what gets in or what gets out.  He only has to decide what gets in the DEFAULT Ubuntu, and then you, the user, can pull in what you wish, be that propriatary or not.  I'm sure if I came and looked at most Ubuntu user's computers, I'd find propriatary software (drivers, codecs, java, flash, mp3, etc.) on them.  Not because you prefer closed source software, but in some cases, it's the only viable option for now.  

We have never suggested, nor would Mark be open to, having the CNR client in Ubuntu by default. 

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=rattaro]Agreed.  As long as there is no "lock in," I don't see an issue at all.  This way, there is real choice.  I think the choice is a great idea.[/QUOTE]

Exactly.

Coke and Pepsi don't "open source" their formula, and I know many people who are "locked in" because they are addicted to their fav cola drink, BUT the thing is, they DO have choice.  There are many other cola drinks to choose from. 

We're not even remotely saying to lock in every Ubuntu user to CNR, FAR FROM IT!  It won't even be in the distro!  We're just asking, is this an OPTION some of you might like to have?  If enough of you say "yes," then would it be right for the rest of you to take that choice and option away from them?  No, it wouldn't, any more than it would be wrong of those who like the idea to force CNR as the lock-in way for Ubuntu.  Just like that would be abhorrent to you, so is the idea of limiting MY freedom to choose offensive to me.

It's just about having a choice.  I don't drink alcohol.  Never have, never will.  I personally choose to not drink.  But, because I think that's what's best for ME, do I try to take that choice away from others?  Never!

As "bad" as I think Microsoft may be, I would be the last person on earth who would try to take that choice away from people.  Leave choice in the hands of the user, and the more options they have, the better, EVEN if I disagree with those choices.   

Kevin

Exactly.

I think it's GREAT that Ubuntu REMAINS 100% open source from the ground up, as it provides for all sorts of distribution opportunities, highlights the value of open source, and so on.  It's great to have options for distros which are 100% open source.  But I would never limit one's choices to what they do with Ubuntu after.  What if a business wanted to migrate totally over to Ubuntu, but they had an internal software program that they had written just for their business which is closed source.  Should they not be able to run this on Ubuntu?  That would be sad.

No one is saying to change the default distro, I'm just asking if CNR is a CHOICE some of you would like to have available to you AFTER you're running it.  That's it.

### Post by nalmeth on 2006-03-03
> lock it down to open source exclusively

Are you really saying that keeping Ubuntu totally open source will for now and forever keep its use restricted? That's the complete opposite point of Ubuntu, and Open Source.  I don't think anyone here 'naively' expects Open Source to knock down closed-source and take over the world tomorrow. We don't hesitate to accept this because it stops a world domination plan. We feel that the future is open source (whenever that future might arrive), and by encouraging the use of closed formats extends that future way ahead in time. Nobody here is against making money for a product, we're against being told what to watch, when to watch it, and how to watch it. We like to support open formats in the hope that at some point our media won't restrict how we use it, and one day its price will be reasonable. 

Of course people need certain closed-source media right now, to get by, but projects that encourage the use of closed-source only spin the tires of the OSS movement in the mud of cyberspace.

Again I have no objection to you offering your service, you can do what you want. But I will not use it, nor will I use linspire, nor will I use Windows.  IT'S NOT WORTH THE MONEY

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Bragador]While CNR is a service for these less skillful people, education is also a service we can offer them.[/QUOTE]

Just because someone CHOOSES NOT to invest hours in learning a new skill just to USE their computer, doesn't mean they are ignorant.  I'm a smart guy, but I have other things I'd prefer to do with my time than having to learn all the technical nuances of Linux.  

Suppose someone gets paid $10 an hour at work.  Unless he can do everything CNR does in LESS than two hours of learning Linux (not likely), then ECONOMICALLY he should just use a service like CNR.  However, SOME people ENJOY tinkering with their computer, so of course, they have other motivations.  But, 98% of the world just wants to USE their computer, not play around with the OS.

Just because someone CHOOSES to pay to have the oil changed in his car does not mean he doesn't know he could learn how to do this himself, or that he's not bright enough to do so.  He may just not WANT to take the time to learn.  So, even if you offer to help a friend learn how to use Linux, don't have your feelings hurt (or assume he's dumb) if he says, "Thanks for the offer, but I have better things to do with my time."

Kevin

### Post by Yagisan on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Kevin Carmony]And as long as you're content with Ubuntu never being used by 98% of the planet, you could be right. 

95% of all people choose their OS at the time they buy their computer, never changing the OS it comes installed with.  How many computer manufacturers are going to ship an OS pre-installed on their computers that won't, from the moment you turn their computer on, work with iPods?  Play MP3s?  View DVDs?  Stream Windows Media files?  Run Java?  Flash?  Not many.[/quote] Where do you get these numbers from ? Are you making them up ? That would be unethical, and misleading wouldn't you agree ?

Just having a quick look through the Ubuntu repositories for breezy, lets see if I can find  the software you claim we are missing, or otherwise unable to use.

Work with iPods -> eg gtkpod
Play MP3s -> eg gstreamer0.8-mad
View DVDs (without CSS) -> xine, totem, or mplayer.
View DVDs (with CSS) -> needs libdvdcss2 (not in repos, unavaliable to U.S.A citizens due to DMCA issues, legal almost anywhere else.)
Stream Windows Media files -> vlc, mplayer
Run Java -> gcj, kaffe, sablevm, j2re1.4 (Blackdown Java, not opensource)
Flash -> libflash-swfplayer, flashplugin-nonfree (Macromedia Flash)

I see it all here. What software are you offering us, that we do not already have in apt ? (I know you offer "codecs", but as only the U.S.A and Japan have decided that maths can now be patented as a "software" patent, what else do you have for the rest of us ?)

[QUOTE=Kevin Carmony]So, if you want to keep Ubuntu to a small percentage of the world, lock it down to open source exclusively.  I'm not saying you don't have a right to do that.  I support Ubuntu's right to do that 100%.  I'm just saying don't be naive and think that the world will be beating a path to your door. 

I think what Mark believes is keep the CORE Ubuntu absolutely remain 100% free of closed source software, BUT to then see Ubuntu spring into dozens of other uses.  If Ubuntu is ever going to find its way onto pre-installs for most computer manufacturers, it will be a built-on-top-of version of Ubuntu that DOES support the things users DEMAND today (mp3, dvd, etc.).

Kevin[/QUOTE]
But, Ubuntu already does all this. Where is the value-add ?

---

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-03
While I agree with Kevin's idea that people can pay for what they don't have time to learn, there are more appropriate analogies than the changing of oil in a car. I've changed the oil in my car and replaced the oil filter myself before--it's a pain in the a**.

Installing applications using Synaptic Package Manager is easy as pie.

It would be like saying, "I'm too lazy to learn how to brush my teeth, so I pay someone to brush them for me," or "I don't have time to tie my shoes, so I pay someone to tie my shoes for me."

I happen to think CNR offers a prettier interface than Synaptic, better descriptions, and screenshots--i.e., it's worthwhile in other ways, but please don't pretend only people who tinker with their computers can use Synaptic Package Manager.

### Post by JeffS on 2006-03-03
As an off and on Ubuntu user (I admit it, I'm a bit of a distro junkie), I think CNR for Ubuntu would be utterly, amazingly fantastic.  It would be a huge win for both Ubuntu and Linspire.

I'm a programmer, tinkerer, IT professional, and general Linux enthusiast who enjoys occasional tinkering with Linux in the command line.  I'm very comfortable with apt-get, both in the command line and with Synaptic.  

That said, I think CNR is completely awesome, and a godsend for newbies/non-technical users, and even technical users like me who still enjoy and appreciate convenience.  And I think CNR is very much worth the cost of the subscription.  With CNR, you are not paying for otherwise free software, you are paying for the convenience of the service.   Apt-get is most certainly one of the greatest things to happen to software management (even compared to Windows software installs).  But CNR is even better.  Much better actually.  CNR embraces and extends apt-get.

Like Kevin Carmony said, it's just like paying a Jiffy Lube to change your oil, even though it is quite possible and not that difficult to do it yourself.

And I think CNR would be terrific for even those seemingly against it.  The convenience of adding all proprietary codecs, flash, java, etc, with one click, and the convenience of having one-click access to commerical software, would be hard to beat.  Automatix is really good, but it does not touch what CNR could bring to the table.  

Sure, some of the more technical users, or more idealistic users (free software only) would not use CNR.  That's fine for them.  But CNR for Ubuntu is nothing but a huge win-win-win for all those concerned.  It would be a huge win for Linspire obviously (who deserve it because they are working their butts off and spending a lot of development dollars on making desktop Linux succeed for the masses), it would be a huge win for Ubuntu (another great convenience/feature available, and instant access to proprietary codecs/formats - a rather difficult stumbling block for newbies), and it would be a huge win for the Linux community at large, as it would sort of unite the efforts of two great distros (one commercial, one community based) who are both focussed on the Linux desktop.

So I say to the posters hear who are not too enthusiastic about this, let it happen if it happens.

And I'd like to thank both Kevin Carmony and Mark Shuttleworth for pursuing this avenue.  I really really really hope it happens.  :D

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-03
First of all, I have to say I'm in favor of Linspire porting CNR to Ubuntu as long as it remains third-party. If Automatix is third-party and Cedega is third-party and Crossover Office is third-party, there's absolutely no reason Mark Shuttleworth or Canonical would make CNR an integral part of Ubuntu.

I believe CNR offers something Synaptic and Gnome-app-install do not currently offer--a prettier interface, more helpful descriptions, and screenshots.

I can't understand how people can compare paying Jiffy Lube to change your oil for you is the same as paying for CNR, for these reasons:

1. When you pay Jiffy Lube to change your oil for you, you pay them to change your oil for you. You do nothing to your car. Nothing. You just drive it there, and they make all the changes for you.

On the other hand, Gnome-app-install and Synaptic Package Manager also install things for you, but you have to click a few times and maybe search for things. CNR also installs things for you, but you have to click a few times and maybe search for things. You do not get any extra convenience for using CNR over SPM. In both cases you're getting something done for you. The only difference is that CNR looks prettier.

If you compared CNR to installing from source, maybe that'd make more sense.

2. Changing oil yourself is a pain. It really is. I've done it on several occasions... when I used to live with my parents and we had a garage. In that garage, I had the proper tools. I had a container to put the used oil. I had to go underneath the car, unscrew the oil tank screw and wait for everything to drain out. I had to find the oil filter (which is *very* difficult to get at in my current car--in fact, I can't get at it at all!) and unscrew that and replace it. Then, I had to put the oil tank screw back and keep checking the oil level with the dipstick as I added new oil. This is a lot of work, I assure you, and it gets you very messy.

Compare that to Synaptic: click, click, click. and CNR: click, click, click.

How is Synaptic like changing your oil yourself? I don't see that.

Synaptic is a graphical application.
It resolves dependencies.
It's searchable.
It offers descriptions of packages.
It's all point-and-click.

CNR is a graphical applcation.
It resolves dependencies.
It's searchable.
It offers descriptions of packages.
It's all point-and-click.

Changing your own oil involves knowledge of the structure of your car.
You have to know where the oil tank is.
You have to have a place to store (at least temporarily) the used oil.
You have to know what oil filter to buy and where it's located in your car.
You have to buy the proper motor oil.
You need rags and possibly a funnel for putting the new oil in.
You need a sink or a shower to clean yourself afterwards.

Paying Jiffy Lube involves no knowledge of the structure of your car.
You pull into the station and say, "I want my oil changed."
You pay them money.

I really don't see how the two are analogous.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=linbetwin]Is CNR going to be included on the Ubuntu CD? Is it going to be in the repos? And if so, which repo?[/QUOTE] Right now, as far as I can tell, it's not "going to" anything yet.

There are different possibilities being discussed, but these are all separate ideas:

1. Linspire might port CNR to Ubuntu.
2. If that happens, there could be a repository where you can get CNR.
3. If so, that may be an additional repository.
4. Some actually want CNR to be standard for Ubuntu, so that you want have to access a repository to get it.

The others are simply non-free and cost money to be distributed (as opposed to obtained). Someone can correct me on this if my understanding of the situation is naive.

### Post by ahood on 2006-03-03
This is certainly an interesting idea, as can be seen by the large number of responses to this thread. Just a couple of comments of my own. *I admit that I have not read every reply in this thread, so if this is a repeat, please accept my apologies.*

**1. Poll** I did cast a vote and I voted for one of the choices. I noticed that voters could vote for multiple choices (not just one). I am not sure why voters have the option of voting for more than one choice. Perhaps voting for multiple choices is the default and an Ubuntu Forum user has no control over it. Nevertheless, because voters can select multiple choices, the meaning of the numbers that this poll generates appears to be difficult to interpret. So, the Ubuntu user comments are what really matters. I guess I should go back and read every one (hehe).

**2. CNR Could Be Good for Ubuntu If.....**

[LIST]
[*]**A. CNR does not break Ubuntu** &#8211; I saw a couple of Ubuntu users express this concern and I agree with it wholeheartedly.

[*]**B. CNR Integrates with Gnome** &#8211; I use Ubuntu because I happen to like the Gnome interface. Although I do have the option of using KDE apps in Gnome, but from what I have heard, this can slow down my system, which I want to avoid. Thus, for me, CNR integrating with Gnome is important.

[*]**C. CNR Adds but Not Replaces** &#8211; In a general sense, it seems that the CNR option is redundant. In other words, do users need another repository from which to obtain software? If the CNR options gives Ubuntu users access to 90% of the software that they already can legally download and use from the Ubuntu repositories, then I don't see a benefit to CNR for Ubuntu. On the other hand, if the CNR option gives Ubuntu users access to software that is **not available** in the Ubuntu repositories (i.e., commercial software like Sun Star Office, Crossover Office, etc.) or legal access to &#8220;restricted software&#8221; (i.e., software that might be illegal for use in certain countries), then there might be some real value to all Ubuntu users. In this second scenario, CNR would not be duplicative because CNR would not contain software that is freely available in the Ubuntu repositories.
[/LIST]

**3. How does CNR Fit in with the Ubuntu Philosophy?**

***Ubuntu Philosophy: that software should be available free of charge, that software tools should be usable by people in their local language and despite any disabilities, and that people should have the freedom to customise and alter their software in whatever way they see fit*** &#8211; see [UbuntuLinux.org](http://www.ubuntulinux.org)

I have not thought through on this one, so I will leave it to the community express their opinion.....

Regards,

Dr. Hood

### Post by roshan.s on 2006-03-03
I have a few questions I hope Mr. Carmony will answer:

1. Will Ubuntu-CNR offer only that software that Ubuntu doesn't already have?

2. How will Ubuntu-CNR integrate with a) dpkg, and b) apt

Lots of posters are confusing **apt** with the **apt repositories**. Apt is simply a way of getting software from the net and installing it on the local computer. It can be both powerful (Synaptic) and simple (gnome-app-install). There's no reason for the existence of a separate paid-and-proprietary "CNR client."

Mr. Carmony gave an example of installing an "improved" firefox through CNR. Now let's say this changed firefox requires newer dependencies than Ubuntu provides. These dependencies also come from CNR. Soon, many packages on the system will be CNR packages, not Ubuntu. At this point, your system is not really Ubuntu anymore. You've lost the benefit of the Ubuntu testing and release cycle.

The other option is to keep CNR-installed packages separate from Ubuntu ones, with their own libraries, etc. At this point, we've gone back to the primitive Windows and Mac OS X style duplication of libs. One great thing about GNU/Linux is that any particular library is stored once on disk and once in RAM.

The power of apt comes when it manages everything on your system. Then it can handle conflicts, dist-upgrades, etc. Here, CNR is operating in parallel with apt and not in cooperation with it, but the only non-trivial "feature" it adds is the billing system. I'm not a web developer and even I can think of several simple ways of adding a billing system to an apt repository, without modifying apt itself. Once again, the power of a package manager is that there is only one. Having two of them defeats the purpose.

I'm afraid CNR on Ubuntu seems to have a not-invented-here syndrome feel to it. It's trying to mold Ubuntu into a CNR distro, instead of adapting itself to the Ubuntu way of doing things. This would be true even if CNR were optional. As soon as you installed CNR, you would start diverging from Ubuntu. Of course a newbie wouldn't understand this, and wouldn't care, but I'm sure the Ubuntu developers would. Of course I could be mistaken, and I'd like someone to correct me.

I feel the best way of implementing CNR would be as an apt repository containing only software not in Ubuntu main or universe. This repository would be optional and would only allow packages to be downloaded from it if payment has been made. Think of it as the PLF repositories, except you must pay to use them. Anybody could mirror the paid repositories, but this would of course be illegal in countries with DMCA-like laws. This is exactly the situation we are in now.

---

### Post by gardion on 2006-03-03
Here is what I understand is being offered. (Kevin please correct me if I'm wrong)
* In order to install proprietery apps in Dapper you will need to enable that option in gnome-app install for Dapper (this option is disabled by default).  So if you don't want proprietery apps you NEVER have to install them or see them.   (Test out live dapper flight 4 to see this)

* IF someone enables proprietery apps THEN they could see a CNR client (a proprietery app) program that could be installed from gnome-app install.   Skype already has this option in dapper and hopefully more will come. 
 
* There is interest in this already since there is a tool called automatix to add new stuff and proprietery stuff onto ubuntu that a lot of people seem to want to use anyways.

* Basically CNR would just make adding some of this stuff easier for people to use.  Therefore attract more users at a cost of $20 a year.

I don't have any issues with this option.  Some people want proprietery apps on Ubuntu and who is any of us to stop them.   In fact I use sun Java.  I'd be tempted to use CNR if they offered some proprietery codecs (and maybe games) myself although probably not the free ones since I can get most of those already in Ubuntu.

I think CNRs main benifit for me is offering me a way of getting proprietery apps and codecs if I want them and a legal way of viewing dvds and mp3s.  The free stuff I can get from Ubuntu.  Of course Newbies may want CNR for everything which I don't mind.

Quite honestly I think the results of the polls are going to be many people will either like the idea or at least not oppose it (since they don't have to use it).  On the other hand there will be a few who will oppose it strongly and not let anyone else use it.    

So we'll end up with this choice.  Not making a choice is kind of like chosing A)

A) Listen to those who oppose it and keep Ubuntu as a small distribution devoted to free software kind of like Ututo and not let people install proprietery apps (I think ubuntu would be a bit of a hypoctrite if it choose this since it already allows proprietery drivers).  This choice will mean keeping the other 98% of the people away from it because ubuntu is missing something they need to work.  This option may also cause some people to look for another distro that is devoted to free software but gives you an easy option to use proprietery apps if you want. (when it comes along).

OR

B) listen to those who support or are indifferent to it and allow it as an option from gnome-app install (if proprietery apps are enabled) like skype in dapper.  This may Alienate small group of users, from Ubuntu who will try another distro (gnubuntu or utotu anyone?).  However, there are many who would support having this choice even if they decide not to use it.  It also would make it easier for people from other OS's to transition to Linux and opensource.  This would then allow us to tap into the other 95%-98% who want things to just work.  

You can tell I'm biased for B.  But Mark Shuttleworth and Kevin Carmony  will need to make a choice of A) or B).  You can't please everyone but you can try to make as many people happy as you can.  That's why I'd support choice B.

### Post by roshan.s on 2006-03-03
Sorry for almost double posting, but there seems to be a lot of confusion about the legality of different codecs and stuff. Feel free to delete this if this is too far off-topic or if it is wrong.

I'm giving the USA as a point of reference since it is the most restricitive jurisdiction. If something is legal in the US, it is probably legal everywhere. IANAL by the way.

1. MP3, AC3 (?) etc. - illegal because of software patents. The algorithm used to decode MP3 is patented and can't be reimplemented in countries with software patents. The EU, India (where I live) and many other places don't have software patents, so it's legal. Nothing to do with DMCA, since no encryption is involved, and nothing to do with copyrights, since the MP3 decoder code is not copied from a company. To make this legal in the USA, you simply need permission from Fraunhofer. Fluendo has already done this and offers a free and legal MP3 gstreamer plugin.

2. DeCSS for DVDs - Illegal only because of DMCA. Commercial DVDs are encrypted and decrypting them is illegal in countries with DMCA-like laws. There's no copyright issue here and probably no patent issue since the DeCSS code is not copied from some commercial company, and CSS is not patented AFAIK. (If it were patented it would not be a secret any longer.)
To allow this in the US, you need to get a decryption key from the DVD Copy Control Association to make a DVD player. DeCSS would be illegal in any case. Linspire might be able to make their own dvd decoder and charge money for it.

3. WMA, Quicktime, etc. - Illegal because of copyrights. There is simply no open-source implementation of this. The only way to get it to run is to copy the compiled decoders from Windows and use them on Linux (w32codecs). This runs into massive copyright problems and is illegal almost everywhere. There's no way Linspire can offer these codecs legally even if they charge for them, unless Microsoft agrees.

Actually, Microsoft has agreed to let Linspire implement Windows Media 8 and 9, but not the DRM bits. [see here]("http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS3080897910.html")

---

(If it were patented it would not be a secret any longer.)
To allow this in the US, you need to get a decryption key from the DVD Copy Control Association to make a DVD player. DeCSS would be illegal in any case. Linspire might be able to make their own dvd decoder and charge money for it.

3. WMA, Quicktime, etc. - Illegal because of copyrights. There is simply no open-source implementation of this. The only way to get it to run is to copy the compiled decoders from Windows and use them on Linux (w32codecs). This runs into massive copyright problems and is illegal almost everywhere. There's no way Linspire can offer these codecs legally even if they charge for them, unless Microsoft agrees.

Actually, Microsoft has agreed to let Linspire implement Windows Media 8 and 9, but not the DRM bits. [see here]("http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS3080897910.html")[/quote]

roshan.s, thanks for the information. It may be offtopic, but it's welcome.

---

gnutux[/QUOTE]
Read the whole thread.  You practically answered your own question.  In addition to working like Synaptic for free software, CNR allows you to easily install commercial software (at least that's what I've gathered - I've never actually used CNR or Linspire personally), one advantage of this that you just identified would be that it enables US users to install proprietary codecs in a fully legitimate manner.

### Post by nixnut on 2006-03-03
I think this is a wonderful idea and very necessary.

With the laws being what they are I don't think there is any other rational way to see distributions like Unbuntu/Kubuntu in BestBuy are any such place equiped with the features that people expect in a computer today. Such as the ability to  play commercial music, dvd's, or produce home movies in a way that can be widely viewed. Without some easy mechanism for managing commercial codecs and in some cases commercial software linux generally and k/ubuntu specifically will never come into widespread use. People I deal with everyday REALLY want this stuff.

I may not like that my own dvd's that I have bought and paid for are essentially being help hostage, but I think it is time the community faces reality in a couple of these areas and responds to it constructively. Breaking laws because I don't like them does not seem like great philosophy to me. This strategy (cnr) seems like a very constructive and non-binding way to deal with the whole issue. It gives choice and features without mandatory lock-in. 

Maybe someday common sense will begin to play a role in IP law again and this whole subject will become an interesting historical note. Until then I need to live in the world I was born in.

### Post by mstlyevil on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=ahood]This is certainly an interesting idea, as can be seen by the large number of responses to this thread. Just a couple of comments of my own. *I admit that I have not read every reply in this thread, so if this is a repeat, please accept my apologies.*

**1. Poll** I did cast a vote and I voted for one of the choices. I noticed that voters could vote for multiple choices (not just one). I am not sure why voters have the option of voting for more than one choice. Perhaps voting for multiple choices is the default and an Ubuntu Forum user has no control over it. Nevertheless, because voters can select multiple choices, the meaning of the numbers that this poll generates appears to be difficult to interpret. So, the Ubuntu user comments are what really matters. I guess I should go back and read every one (hehe).

**2. CNR Could Be Good for Ubuntu If.....**

[LIST]
[*]**A. CNR does not break Ubuntu** – I saw a couple of Ubuntu users express this concern and I agree with it wholeheartedly.

[*]**B. CNR Integrates with Gnome** – I use Ubuntu because I happen to like the Gnome interface. Although I do have the option of using KDE apps in Gnome, but from what I have heard, this can slow down my system, which I want to avoid. Thus, for me, CNR integrating with Gnome is important.

[*]**C. CNR Adds but Not Replaces** – In a general sense, it seems that the CNR option is redundant. In other words, do users need another repository from which to obtain software? If the CNR options gives Ubuntu users access to 90% of the software that they already can legally download and use from the Ubuntu repositories, then I don't see a benefit to CNR for Ubuntu. On the other hand, if the CNR option gives Ubuntu users access to software that is **not available** in the Ubuntu repositories (i.e., commercial software like Sun Star Office, Crossover Office, etc.) or legal access to “restricted software” (i.e., software that might be illegal for use in certain countries), then there might be some real value to all Ubuntu users. In this second scenario, CNR would not be duplicative because CNR would not contain software that is freely available in the Ubuntu repositories.
[/LIST]

**3. How does CNR Fit in with the Ubuntu Philosophy?**

***Ubuntu Philosophy: that software should be available free of charge, that software tools should be usable by people in their local language and despite any disabilities, and that people should have the freedom to customise and alter their software in whatever way they see fit*** – see [UbuntuLinux.org](http://www.ubuntulinux.org)

I have not thought through on this one, so I will leave it to the community express their opinion.....

Regards,

Dr. Hood[/QUOTE]

I made the poll with multiple choice because there are a number of people who would support CNR for Ubuntu but not actually use it themselves. It gives people a clearer voice to express how they personally would use CNR. I explained this in the opening post and was hoping people would first read the first post before voting in the poll.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=gnutux]I don't think it's a great idea,  since why would we want to buy free (as in beer) software like how Linspire users are required to in order to get the free software.

gnutux[/QUOTE]

1. Ease of use.

You may be technical enough to appreciate apt, but most people have no interest in spending time learning new tricks.  The easier things are to use, the less time they have to spend fussing about.

2. Legal use.

Some people don't want to break laws or find it unethical and wrong to steal IP from others.  Maybe they own an iPod, or they want to view movie trailers on the web in Windows Media.  etc.

If you're happy with things as they are, great!  But 98% of the rest of the world would just scratch their head, look at you funny, and turn their Windows computer on.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Lord Illidan]He could. But that would hurt Ubuntu directly, wouldn't it? As newcomers would probably chose that over CNR + Ubuntu.

I mean, if you buy an OS just for CNR, you can use Linspire. But Linspire costs more money. So people can get a free OS, Ubuntu, and if they want, they can get extra service for 20$ a year, which I don't think is a lot of money. It is far less then Cedega charges you to play games.[/QUOTE]

And that's what happens today.  Linspire has over 350 OEM's who pre-install Linspire on their computers, and these PCs are [being sold by]("http://www.linspire.com/featured_partner/featured_partner.php?sent=1&country=1") the likes of Sears, Wal-Mart, Fry's, Micro Center, K-Mart, Tiger Direct, and others.  Ubuntu has only a handful of OEM's.  Why?  Because 95% of the world doesn't buy an operating system, THEY BUY A COMPUTER.  Most people just use the OS that comes on their computer when they buy it.  So, if you want to sell more computers, you put the best OS on it you can for the money.  Why would an OEM use Ubuntu?  "Hey, buy my computer, sure you can't play MP3's on it, watch DVD's, look at Windows Media movie trailers, etc., but with a few days of training and breaking a law or two, you can!  Just go ahead and toss that iPod and use OGG."  

By allowing for AN OPTION of brining more ease of use and better functionality to Ubuntu, you also allow more people to use it, including OEM's.

Kevin

### Post by roshan.s on 2006-03-03
Mark Shuttleworth's commercial interests are limited to commercial services, not products. CNR as a service is a good idea, since packaging is a time consuming job and people might want to pay for extra package repositories.

What I find worrying about CNR is that it requires a proprietary software PRODUCT to be installed and used. Why doesn't CNR integrate with apt? Apt is certainly [not difficult]("http://loktarogar.blogspot.com/2006/03/new-gnome-app-install-for-ubuntu-604.html") to [use]("http://www.flickr.com/photos/jsgotangco/107106644/").

My basic worry is that Ubuntu-CNR will sacrifice the Ubuntu-ness of Ubuntu to make it easier for Linspire developers, instead of working seamlessly with the rest of the Ubuntu package system which Ubuntu developers are working hard to streamline.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=SFN]If Ubuntu has CNR, why would someone buy Linspire? They could just install Ubuntu (avoiding the whole running as root thing) and have CNR.[/QUOTE]

Running as root thing?  Will some FUD never die???  Linspire is the same as every other distro.  When you install it, you set up users.  The ONLY version of Linspire that didn't allow for this was the VERY FIRST "sneak preview" that was only available for testers FOUR YEARS AGO.  It was never a commercially available product, but just the early draft as we were building Linspire.  Every single commercially available version of Linspire has allowed for users.  

As for why someone would still use Linpsire, it's because Ubuntu and Linspire are still VERY different products.  Linspire is about ease of use from top to bottom.  Does Ubuntu have narrated tutorials telling you how to surf the web?  Linspire does.  Ubuntu would never do that, because it's not targeted at new users, as Linspire is.  85% of the homes in Mexico don't own a computer, and Linspire PCs do very well there because they are so affordable ($199ish).  Many of these people in Mexico who are buying a Linspire PC have never owned a computer before, so they need hand holding to surf the web, let alone learn how to use apt, etc.  So, even with CNR, Linspire still has a very different focus on ease of use than Ubuntu does.

Next, there is branding.  Linspire's brand is known for 1) OEM's, 2) Ease of Use, 3) Consumers.  That is not Ubuntu's focus.  How much different is Arrow Head water from Evian?  The product isn't much different (it's water!), but the marketing and branding are.

95% of the world is using Windows today.  I think there is plenty of room for SEVERAL Linux projects/companies.  Linspire thinks MORE choice is good.  Ubuntu is a cousin, not some "competitor" to be held back.  The better Ubuntu does, the better the entire Linux space does, and the better for Linspire.  

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=TeeAhr1]Regarding your comment about Ubuntu and apt: apt-get has a graphical interface, called Synaptic.  It's very user-friendly, divides stuff up into categories for easy browsing, has a search function.  I'm a huge fan of it.  I include a screenshot for your viewing pleasure.[/QUOTE]

Yes, that's nicer than a command line, but I think if you look at CNR, you'll still see a big difference.

Kevin

[IMG]http://media.linspire.com/images/screenshots/cnr_welcome_800.jpg[/IMG]

[IMG]http://media.linspire.com/images/screenshots/cnr_warehouse_800.jpg[/IMG]

Just some of the differences CNR has....
- One click install (and uninstall and updating)
- Dedicated, high-speed server farms with huge bandwidth pipes for fast downloads
- Icons are added to the desktop and Launch menu
- Friendly names (new users don't know what a "package" is)
- Screenshots of programs
- User reviews of the programs
- Voting booth where you can vote to have new software added or updated
- Publisher program where ANYONE can add software to the warehouse in an automated fashion
- Automatic update notification and one click updating of OS AND applications
- Service manager built in as well for things like web filtering, wallpaper delivery service, etc.
- Aisles to group applications for one-click install of them all
- Supports "buying" commercial programs, such as StarOffice, DVD Player, Crossover, Transgaming, etc.
- Automatically retries and fixes any wedged downloads.
- 98%+ success rate (CNR has a better than 98% success rate, and the less than 2% of failures are usually a result of modem drop offs, in which case CNR will can automatically resume when connected, providing the user with a successful install.)
- Enhanced applications that have been improved before adding to Warehouse
- Support from full-time trained and qualified staff

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Kevin Carmony]Running as root thing?  Will some FUD never die???  Linspire is the same as every other distro.  When you install it, you set up users.  The ONLY version of Linspire that didn't allow for this was the VERY FIRST "sneak preview" that was only available for testers FOUR YEARS AGO.  It was never a commercially available product, but just the early draft as we were building Linspire.  Every single commercially available version of Linspire has allowed for users.[/QUOTE] I noticed your careful use of the word *allowed*. Nobody said Linspire doesn't *allow* the addition of users. It does, however, *default* to a root account, thus encouraging people to run as root.

The ability to add non-root users is considered an "Advanced configuration" option at the end of the Linspire install.

### Post by dolson on 2006-03-03
After much consideration, and reading every post in this thread since the last one I made, I want to change my original knee-jerk votes on the poll.

I support Linspire offering value-added services OUTSIDE of Ubuntu's software repositories.

This is taking up a lot of his time, I know because I spent over an hour myself just now reading posts as fast as I can.

I am not opposed to seeing Dell or HP or IBM or Walmart selling Ubuntu + CNR for a user-friendly OOTB experience. I just wish that it wasn't a pay-as-you-go service, but this is the business model of the future, I guess.

Anyhow, so my vote is that I won't use it and don't want it in Ubuntu, but I think Linspire has nothing to lose by offering it as a 100% independant service that new users and lazy users will be willing to pay for. Although, I think the details should be worked out more, as there already are NVIDIA drivers and the like in Ubuntu's repos, so focus only on things that we can't already get.

Kevin, ignore the haters. And I would love to see Ubuntu+CNR PCs sitting on store shelves or offered online from the big players. I would buy a PC with Ubuntu+CNR myself (although I would wipe it down and reinstall from scratch).

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=aysiu]I noticed your careful use of the word *allowed*. Nobody said Linspire doesn't *allow* the addition of users. It does, however, *default* to a root account, thus encouraging people to run as root.[/QUOTE]

Not sure what you mean by default?  During the install you're running as root, as you need to be doing so to add users.

It goes like this...

1. Put in install CD.
2. Linspire has you create an Admin password then installs the OS files.
3. Linspire then reboots and brings up a "first run Wizard" where you set up your users and other options (the screen shot you have there).
4. Linspire then restarts and asks you how you want to login, as Admin or User.

Kevin

### Post by Bragador on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=*John*]There is one thing that I *really* dont understand......

What draws the majority of people to Open Source software (IMHO) is that its free as in beer, and free as in freedom.

Is the whole Open Source movement really able to include propriatry software into the mix without putting people off?

I'm a good example of this, if i'm going to pay for my software, i'd rather buy Microsoft/Apple software. I am here because of Ubuntu's ideology, and the fact that its free, there is no cost to me giving Linux a try, if I dont like it, then i'ts cost me nothing?

What i'm getting at is, the only thing noobs really know for sure about Linux is that it, and its software is free. If they come here and start seeing subscription add-ons, arnt they (wrongly) going to think, this is just cheaper than MS/Apple, not free? If i'm going to pay for something i'd rather pay for something I KNOW will suit my needs, and has the broadest compatability?

John \\:D/[/QUOTE]


Exactly what I'm thinking.

I digg that

### Post by jobezone on 2006-03-03
Hi, I voted for the 2nd option, although it did not completely represent my opinion. Although it would be fine for me if Linspire made a CNR for Ubuntu users, I don't really agree that Canonical and Ubuntu Foundation should give it any kind of special support.

The reason is that, for me, freedom in computing matters, a lot, and for Ubuntu to support more ways to install non-free software than it already currently does, would be a step back. For many reasons, one of which is that Ubuntu is one of the most visible and oustpoken "distributions" which is outspoken about freedom in computing.

There are still pratical incoveniences to using a 100% free system, but the most important and limiting ones right now are closed formats, proprietary codecs, and the dumbness of software patents and USA's DMCA (and in the future, DRM). This makes it legally impossible to freely distribute wmv,quicktime,mp3,dvdcss,flash, and java. Hope I'm not missing any important other. 

With respect with the wmv, quicktime, and mp3 codecs, I would say "vote with your feet". Don't support them. Don't make your videos and music on any of these formats (use ogg vorbis and ogg theora, for example). Only buy a gadget media player if it supports these formats (yep, that leaves ipod out). These codecs are patent free, licensed under a bsd-like license, and encoders and decoders for them are natively supported in ubuntu, any other linux distribution, and natively or via a plugin by windows and OSX players.

The remaining ones are flash and java: alternatives for these are being developed (GCJ, and various flash projects). 

To finish, I myself need to have some of the previous ones installed to fully enjoy my computer (specifically, I have flash, java and libdvdcss installed. That means no w32codecs, people :) I'll yet have to see for how long can I keep me from installing these) but I'm also strict on allways making videos, musics, documents, etc., which don't force other people to do the same as I have.

So, my point is:
I think the current "RestrictedFormats" wikipage on ubuntu is suficient to both help users get these things and explain them why and how restricted are them.
Linspire with CNR, and any others, are welcome to support Ubuntu, but this shouldn't go the other way around, Ubuntu supporting CNR.

Hope the text is not too rambling and confusing...

---

Ubuntu will always be free of charge, and there is no extra fee for the "enterprise edition", we make our very best work available to everyone on the same Free terms.[/B]

**"Ubuntu is entirely committed to the principles of free software development; we encourage people to use free and open source software, improve it and pass it on." **

A thread with an idea like this would not have been seriously considered when Mark Shuttleworth's generous vision of a different world for the future began this project. Its users would have seen this as not merely "a question of choice" (in package managers/software) but rather a fundamental change of principles. Ubuntu users would be able to see past the "it's only 20 dollars" to understand that no, it's much more expensive than that. Commitments and principles like the ones on the front page of Ubuntu Linux are priceless.

Nothing. Not including what vista is apparently gonna have.[/QUOTE]

Because Microsoft controls what software comes by default and they have shown a talent for making their applications "core" to the Windows operating system so they can't be removed, peoples choices are limited.  Take for example the Firefox browser, Microsoft's Internet Explorer is irremovable, so given enough time a significant proportion of people will go back to it, not because they want to, but because they are reinstalling Windows and new version of Internet Explorer can do most of the things that Firefox can, so they will exhibit that most predicable of human behaviour laziness.  In other words with Windows you only have choice if you can constantly be bothered to grasp it, this gives far to much power to a company that after all is only really trying to serve the interests of its shareholders.

*John* as far as Ubuntu being free, it is!  

However it isn't free from proprietary software (at least not on my machine).  What I want is an easy legal way of getting hold of the things which aren't available direct from Ubuntu.  This is something that **I am willing to pay for**, maybe Ubuntu don't want to provide these for moral reasons, but I would be surprised and disappointed if a significant proportion of Linux users wanted to stop me from being able to this.

### Post by mstlyevil on 2006-03-03
Since there has been some confusion on how to vote on the poll because it is multiple choice and a few changed minds after voting I decided to go through every post and document who wnats the choice of CNR being offered. Here is what I did. I scanned through each post and wrote down each persons name. Then I used this criteria to decide who was in favor of having the choice for CNR. They must make clear that they are not against the choice for CNR to be offered even if they personally will not use it. If they are not clear in that choice then I left the line next to their name blank. I double checked I did not repeat any poster and if repeated then I just crossed out the repeat. If they changed their minds anywhere in the thread I put a check in their line and changed their answer at the end to reflect their current feelings. Here are the results from people who actually cared enough to post their opinion.

There were 114 actual posters.

1) 90 were for people having a choice to use CNR as a third party application.

2) 13 were absolutely against having any choice in Ubuntu whatsoever

3) 11 just did not make their choices clear

4) 2 people changed their minds

I believe this number is a better representation of how most of the Ubuntu Community feels about this.

Kevin, I hope you will take this survey into consideration when making a final decision and feel free to check my numbers because I think you will find I was very close in figuring my numbers.

### Post by gardion on 2006-03-03
> Originally Posted by weekend warrior
Oh dear, I appear to be lost. It's very possible as I haven't posted for some time. Please tell me, is this still in fact the Ubuntu project forums? The one that proudly states on its front page:

"The team behind Ubuntu makes the following public commitment to its users:"

Ubuntu will always be free of charge, and there is no extra fee for the "enterprise edition", we make our very best work available to everyone on the same Free terms.

"Ubuntu is entirely committed to the principles of free software development; we encourage people to use free and open source software, improve it and pass it on."

Or has it changed into something not entirely unlike the raging horde of commercial ventures in the world today? Because from the sounds of this, there will indeed be a defacto "enterprise edition". Last I was here, Ubuntu was a special project for the entire world, rich and poor, on both sides of the digital divide; a project that made committments to its users and stood firm by them, much as Debian has done for over a decade.

No no.... I couldn't possibly be on the right page. A thread with an idea like this would not have been seriously considered when Mark Shuttleworth's generous vision of a different world for the future began this project. Its users would have seen this as not merely "a question of choice" (in package managers/software) but rather a fundamental change of principles. Ubuntu users would be able to see past the "it's only 20 dollars" to understand that no, it's much more expensive than that. Commitments and principles like the ones on the front page of Ubuntu Linux are priceless.

So pray tell, are these pages the Ubuntu forums? The ones that I once knew and respected? Or have I inadvertently stumbled across the forums for Ubuntu Ltd. - the Enterprise Edition - a division of Linspire Inc. ?

If so, my heart is saddened by the loss.


I think some of you are confused about what is going on here.  Ubuntu proper, what gets included on the cds and repositories will always be free from what I can see.  The default installation will never include any proprietery software.  In fact, Ubuntu could not be freely distributed if it included proprietery software since that would limit it's distribution since free software licences are fare more liberal then proprietery ones!  

So if you will the ubuntu platform will always be free as far as I can see and this idea is not changing it.  However, if someone wants to put a proprietery APPLICATION on Ubuntu who are we to stop them?   

I think what some of you missed was this goal

**"Ubuntu will include the very best in translations and accessibility infrastructure that the Free Software community has to offer, to make Ubuntu usable by as many people as possible. We collaborate as widely as possible on bug fixing and code sharing."**

One of ubuntu's goals is to make "ubuntu as usuable by as many people as possible."  I think this also means we have to make it accessible to people who write proprietery software and let people use it if they want.  However, that software should never be included with Ubuntu itself and the defaults should always be free.  This is the best way to encourage people to use free software is by having high quality free software as the default included software on the desktop.  Remember how Internet Explorer got popular?  MS included it on their desktop as part of the default install and it practically wiped Netscape of the face of the earth. 

The proprietery software is **3rd party**.  Not part of ubuntu.  Skype is 3rd party software not part of Ubuntu.  Flash is 3rd party, not part of ubuntu.  Sun Java 3rd party and not part of Ubuntu.  Ubuntu is a platform for people to build on.  CNR is simply a proprietary application that allows you to install proprietery applications (or legal codecs).  Technically you can also install free applications but I think their are better ways for installing free software.


> 
With over 1000 distro's why do you think we need "cross distro propriatry software compatability" (if that makes sense  ) to maintain "choice"?

Ubuntu is free, and until now, I was sure freedom from propriatry software was one of its principals, but now I feel I may have been mistaken/decieved?

If you want what Linspire is offering then whats wrong with Linspire? Its like saying I want to use the apple Safari browser on XP? If I want that so badly, I have to buy a mac!


Ubuntu + CNR is incredibly different from a Mac.  CNR is an APPLICATION, the Mac GUI is part of the OS or Apple PLATFORM.  There is a huge difference.  

I don't think CNR will ever become part of the Ubuntu platorm.  However, it will be a nice APPLICATION for people who want legal ways of installing codecs and easy access to third party proprietery applcations.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=*John*]Is the whole Open Source movement really able to include propriatry software into the mix without putting people off?[/QUOTE]

2% might be put off.  I'd argue, however, for the other 98% of the world, THEY ALREADY ARE PUT OFF because open source won't work with their DVD's, iPods, play Windows Media movie trailers, support Java, Flash, etc. without them learning a bunch of new stuff, spending hours getting things to work, and probably breaking a few laws along the way.

If Linux is ever going to be an option for "the rest of the world," then for now anyway, it MUST interoperate with the realities of technology today.  

The way it is right now, open source is like selling a new ecology friendly car that runs on some new fuel.  You can tell people, "This new car is great!  It's reliable, fast, costs less to operate, but...you have to drive 200 miles every time you want to fill it up, because it doesn't use the same gas the rest of the world does."  Some would buy that car, but most wouldn't.  

So, until open source has enough software to replace all the codecs, drivers, applications, etc. most people need today, why not interoperate with it FOR NOW?  If Linux wants to really change the world, get 20% of the world using it!  Some would argue that if you give in, it will never change.  I'd argue if you don't, it won't either.  The rest of the world is NOT going to embrace OGG just because 1% of the Linux techies do.  (In fact, most Linux techies still use MP3 =)  

Kevin

---

### Post by KiwiNZ on 2006-03-03
[quote=Kevin Carmony]2% might be put off.  I'd argue, however, for the other 98% of the world, THEY ALREADY ARE PUT OFF because open source won't work with their DVD's, iPods, play Windows Media movie trailers, support Java, Flash, etc. without them learning a bunch of new stuff, spending hours getting things to work, and probably breaking a few laws along the way.

If Linux is ever going to be an option for "the rest of the world," then for now anyway, it MUST interoperate with the realities of technology today.  

The way it is right now, open source is like selling a new ecology friendly car that runs on some new fuel.  You can tell people, "This new car is great!  It's reliable, fast, costs less to operate, but...you have to drive 200 miles every time you want to fill it up, because it doesn't use the same gas the rest of the world does."  Some would buy that car, but most wouldn't.  

So, until open source has enough software to replace all the codecs, drivers, applications, etc. most people need today, why not interoperate with it FOR NOW?  If Linux wants to really change the world, get 20% of the world using it!  Some would argue that if you give in, it will never change.  I'd argue if you don't, it won't either.  The rest of the world is NOT going to embrace OGG just because 1% of the Linux techies do.  (In fact, most Linux techies still use MP3 =)  

Kevin[/quote]

Very good point(s)

---

### Post by americymru on 2006-03-03
I have now read fifty pages of this stuff and I feel the need to get on with my life so heres my two cents worth.

My initial reaction to the idea was that it would be beneficial in terms of encouraging Ubuntu adoption.

My position 50 pages later?..... Exactly the same.

If I understand the nay-sayers correctly they are concerned that this might be the thin end of a rather unpleasant wedge. It might lead to further commercialisation of the distro etc. I think this is very unlikely but even if it were true , there are other places to go. I am very pleased with Ubuntu at the moment and use it for all my day to day computing needs. BUT...if it were to start developing in a direction which I found disagreeable I coul;d always go back to SUSE, or Mandriva, or EVEN Fedora Core.

Soooooo....whilst I agree that we must continue to keep a very wary eye on developments of this kind I continue to support Mr Carmony's proposal.

Will I use it myself? Yes....I will use it when doing installs for noobs and I will use it for proprietary software downloads.

P.S. Thanks Kevin, for provoking the liveliest debate i've seen on Ubuntu Forum.

### Post by weekend warrior on 2006-03-03
The larger issue which many of you fail to see or don't wish to see is right on the front page of Ubuntu Linux, again:  

**"we encourage people to use free and open source software, improve it and pass it on."**

Should we as the Ubuntu community be encouraging users to simply use Linux as a "mere platform" for closed, patented, restricted proprietary software? This is the issue, not whether it's bundled in the core (which it obviously never would be) or a 3rd party application. 

Let me ask you this. If Photoshop could have been easily plopped on top of our mere linux platform would the free and open alternatives like the GIMP and Inkscape exist? If Adobe Reader were one click away for most users would Evince have ever come about? How about iTunes? Would we really have need for our own free and open media players like Rhythmbox or Amarok? And so on and so on through a multitude (just about all in fact) of fine free and open alternatives that speak to the true identity of Linux as a continually evolving, improving, ever more complete **open and free** OS, not just a mere "platform" for hosting closed proprietary code that we, the community **cannot improve** nor pass on. because it's not ours, it's theirs. Do you agree with the EULA? Click yes to continue. Do you have a choice?

Ubuntu Linux has done much better than other distros at promoting this ideal of Linux, to be a true alternative to closed, patented software. Other distros have not gone this way, have made it all too easy to load on heaps of closed software. Well here's another question. Doesn't OS X already have that covered fairly well? An open platform with a proprietary veneer? That's what it is after all. The question is, is that all we want Linux to be too?

---

### Post by jarocooke on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=midwinter]I think if closed source software had always been as prominent in Linux as in other OS' we wouldn't have the great sharing community Linux is known for.  But that's me.  

I also do not feel Linux is being held back in any way, either though.[/QUOTE]

I agree that proprietary software shouldn't be allowed to take over, as it probably would have at the beginning, but this isn't the early days!

The only way to really change anything is to break the virtual monopoly of Windows.  If Firefox hadn't been available for Windows then there wouldn't be anything like the pressure on Web designers to make sure that there websites are compatible with it, which would impact on my web experience since I am not using IE.

What I am saying is, I think that allowing a greater uptake by supporting a simple legal way to do the things that most normal computer users want to do, can only be a good thing for OSS as it will increase its install base.

Those that don't want to use proprietary software will be no worse off, but the rest of us will have a much wider choice of proprietary games ported to GNU/Linux.;)  Not to mention the fact that we will stand a much better chance of convincing more traditionally proprietary software companies to embrace what I believe to be a fundamentally superior development model.

### Post by jarocooke on 2006-03-03
[QUOTE=Bragador]But then are you saying the goal of linux is to become more like Mac OS ? Partly free and partly owned ?

Linux is building itself slowly because the rich guys don't want to change their business model. Change is something most humans fear. Instead of giving what the market wants, just educate your friends and family on what computers could be and THEN explain what linux is.[/QUOTE]

Mac OS is a different kettle of fish because the operating system is tied to the hardware and the proprietary component comprises the whole GUI, not just codecs and applications that run on top of the operating system.

In fact my wife and mother in law are both using Ubuntu.  However my parents are proving more difficult to convert.  They run a professional photography company and require photoshop to be natively supported in GNU/Linux before they can even consider it.  -- I rest my case for the need to support proprietary software in GNU/Linux, they want to embrace it, but the realities of running a successful business are preventing them.:cry:

So to cut a long story short, I think that GNU/Linux needs companies like Linspire to provide temporary solutions and to encourage other software vendors to play ball.  We can only do that if we gang up, and we gang up by sending money in the direction of those companies that help to solve our little missing "bits", so I would rather pay Linspire for codecs and DVD players than use legally dubious free versions.

### Post by matthinckley on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=jobezone]I'll just point out that there are people who care, moreso than I do: the ones who develop all the free alternatives to that software, like the ogg theora, gcj,etc. As users, we too can do our part, with very little inconvenience, by _not_ buying mp3-only gadgets, and by disseminating music and movies using free codecs.
As far as ipod, there are many ogg-supporting players out there, with better features, and cheaper in general.

And if the question was wether ubuntu users would mind if CNR supported ubuntu, well my obvious answer is, you don't need my permission, and I think no one can object to what you decide to support or not. As the question was being explained, it seemed that it entailed more than this, like some aknowledgement or support from ubuntu's side.

Was this some kind of PR? ;)[/QUOTE]

I totally agree with your post and fully support the open-source movement.  I don't own any mp3-only gadgets myself and probably wouldn't buy one.

but...  what about my friend that comes over with *his* iPod and wants to put a song on my computer.. should I just tell him.. oh I can't use this on my linux computer because it uses closed-source proprietary codecs that I would have to pay for?  You may say yes, but I would rather have the ability to play that mp3 on my computer, then if I wanted to, I could convert it into OGG.  Or how about the friend that has the mp3-only player that really wants one of the songs off of my computer?  should I tell him he can't play my music on his player because it doesn't support open-source codecs?  again you may say yes, but I would rather let him have the song and then, *if he is interested*, explain to him how I use open-source formats and why it is good to support them. 

My point is that if all you can tell closed-source users (i.e. the majority of consumer electonics users) is that their systems will not interoperate with yours, why would any of them want to switch over?  as I'm sure you know being an open-source supporter, converting a large collection of music, movies, documents, spreadsheets, etc.. over to open-source formats from closed-source would take a lot of somebody's time.. so why would any non-technical user want to make the switch when everything they have works together nicely the way it is now?

I understand the benefits of open-source but that's not the whole reason I switched to linux.  I switched because I like the ability to tinker with my operating system.. My codecs being proprietary or not makes no consequense to me.  I just want my video to work.  and my sound. and I want to be able to share my music collection with my friends.  If you were to tell me that in order to use linux only with open source formats I wouldn't be here.. I would still be using windows, and probably not feeling like I was missing out on anything important because I would believe that if I was to use linux I wouldn't be able to hook up my friends iPod or view or send my friends word documents or excel spreadsheets.. 

everybody complains about microsoft locking people into closed-source formats, I completely agree but then some of the same people complaining about it want to lock people into open-source formats and I don't get it.

I say make CNR available for those who want it, and for those who don't: you have the **freedom** not to install it!

Matt

---

These are issues about friendship, and praticality. It's not legal to distribute those codecs without the licensors permissions, and in some countries to even distribute free software like dvdcss. It also restricts us all in many other different ways. Nevertheless we can install it, but the problem still exists. And then the dilema appears: How to switch to a new format, when everyone else is using a different one? And I answer, slowly :) . 
Me, personally, I'd be willing to give someone the winxp cd that came with my laptop if he ultimately needed it.
>

What I would prefer is that current linux/free system users start migrating to these free formats, and get them dessiminated. It's happening now (see opendocument format being adopted by many governments and users), happened before (PNG replaced GIF in mostly all free software programs and websites), and can happen again (ogg).
> I understand the benefits of open-source but that's not the whole reason I switched to linux.  I switched because I like the ability to tinker with my operating system.. everybody complains about microsoft locking people into closed-source formats, I completely agree but then some of the same people complaining about it want to lock people into open-source formats and I don't get it.

Wow. By "locking" yourself into open-source/free software or free formats avoid you being locked into a system, program, company, etc.
> 
I say make CNR available for those who want it, and for those who don't: you have the **freedom** not to install it!

Agreed, like it already happens with any other piece of proprietary software we can have in ubuntu (unless DRM become prevalent in the future, of course).

### Post by matthinckley on 2006-03-04
I don't see how Linspire creating a warehouse (which I assume to mean a CNR exclusive repository) that would carry consistently updated open-source software as well as closed-source software and charging ubuntu users a minimal fee to have access to this warehouse is freeloading.  I think that people are under the misconception that you are paying $20/year for the client program.. I don't see how that is the case.. you can download and install the program on your computer for 'free' from either a website or repository.  what you are paying the $20/year fee for is access to the CNR warehouse.  Why is this so bad?

A 3rd party application such as the one proposed here for Ubuntu would only serve to undermine the chosen ideals and vision of the creator and developers of this distro in the same way that posting in the Linspire forums about a 3rd party app to covert CNR to an absoultely free service would undercut theirs and it's not appreciated by this particular Ubuntu denizen.
