### Post by weekend warrior on 2006-03-04
I have no desire to restrict anyone's choices. I couldn't even if I wanted to as in the end, it's not up to me, after all. Kevin Carmony came onto this forum to ask for opinions as per a port of CNR for Ubuntu and I've given my opinion as to why I don't like the idea. You are as free to your opinions as I am to mine.

As an aside though, I honestly wonder if one were to open a like-minded thread/poll on the Linspire forums if it would even be permitted or rather closed down. Whence my satirical suggestion earlier.

As for the 'logical conclusion' I was considering to post 541, it would be to make the GIMP better than Pixel. To allow free and open software the opportunity to continue to prove itself, to develop, to grow, to not be stifled and snuffed out (by raucous corporate marketing, misleading perceptions or what have you) before it has its chance because there are many people the world over who put their blood, sweat and tears into these projects; many who have placed their very lifeblood into them. Please take the time to consider them as well.

Open source has given us an immense amount of goodness. Given time and patience. I'm confident the GIMP could be every bit as good or better than a closed, proprietary solution (if given a chance and supported by us). I also believe that if the tools we currently have in opensource are not up to the tasks and features offered by CNR, then they should be looked at to see how to improve them for everyone before looking to a closed code solution.


(p.s. my apolgies go out to you for any condescending tone you may have felt Master Shake)

Linux has a lot of corporate backing now and it is generating billions in revenue every year.
So there's no need to worry and no need to place artificial barriers in the way of propietary software just so that free software can survive. Free software is strong enough to survive without this and has shown this time and time again.

### Post by weekend warrior on 2006-03-04
No *John*, you're not so much in conflict, it's simply the case that this issue has been needlessly cornered into an overly-simplistic yes/no argument when it is in fact a question of priorities and preferences which present a much more finely grained issue that more often than not, must be examined on a case by case basis. 

The priorities and preferences that the Ubuntu Linux project hold to have been lain out publicly on the front page of the website in the form of commitments to its users. They do not say.... "proprietary software = bad" or "you musn't/cannot use it, ever". However given the choice, the preference for the Ubuntu creator and devs is quite clear. 

Open source is encouraged over closed source. There are no restrictions on choices here, rather preferences and goals for the future. Goals which as I understand them are simply different here as opposed to Linspire and that may present certain tangible and intangible incompatibilities in terms of distro cooperation.

A small digression here, has CNR been presented to the Debian project? If so, what was their reaction? After all, why come to us? Ubuntu needs to synch with Debian every six months, right? If it *hasn't* been offered to the Debian community, why not? 


In closing, a project that has preferences and priorities and has taken the time to emphatically write out commitments to the community does not limit your choices, but it *does* present you with a certain amount of personal responsibility if you believe and partake in the project's goals.

---

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=weekend warrior]No *John*, you're not so much in conflict, it's simply the case that this issue has been needlessly cornered into an overly-simplistic yes/no argument when it is in fact a question of priorities and preferences which present a much more finely grained issue that more often than not, must be examined on a case by case basis.

Your comments on this have been both insightful and thought provoking.

If i'm honest it is my concern that eventually, even a well intentioned organisation like Ubuntu may be compromised by the mix of closed source proprietary software, and open source projects.

If that happens, although Kevin Carmoney may well trumpet this a clear victory for "choice" and "freedom", we are then presented with a computing world of Microsoft, Apple, and Linux all doing exactly the same things.

Right now, Linux is able to differentiate itself as a beacon for freedom, and open source principals. If we allow that to be compromised, Linux is no better or worse than Microsoft/Apple...... it "joins the herd".

As I have already said, there is actual proof that Linux and proprietary software and Linux dont mix.... LINSPIRE! On the face of it, Linspire is a great OS, but why is it not selling? Because it isnt free as in beer, nor free as in freedom!

John

### Post by Master Shake on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=weekend warrior]

(p.s. my apolgies go out to you for any condescending tone you may have felt Master Shake)[/QUOTE]

And I apologize for snapping, as this thread has been very wearying..

But to continue...

I like the Gimp.  It really isn't a bad program.  However, it does not meet my needs.  If someone is going to give me what I want, I'll go out of my way to (legally) obtain that product.  Pixel, from what I've used DOES offer what I want, and it offers it now.  If this were time-critical, I couldn't sit around waiting for the Gimp to catch up, just because its open source.

Yes, I'd love to use all open source, but the fact is that just because a product is "open source" doesn't mean its a GOOD open source product (I can think of several games in the repository off the top of my head).  My needs are my #1 consideration, cost is #2, and OSS is #3.  Ubuntu meets all those needs for me.  To be honest, while I support CNR being made available for ubuntu, I may not actually use it.  I just think its not right to have that choice taken from us. 

 Governments take choices from us all the time, and what good has that gotten us?

EDIT:  FWIW, I use AbiWord on my Win partition (as well as my Linux) because IMHO its FAR superior to word.  The OSS / Proprietary argument goes both ways.

### Post by Bragador on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=weekend warrior]I have an idea. I'd like to know what people here think about this before going to ask Linspire customers their opinion.

Wouldn't it be a splendid idea to create a 3rd party application that would make all of CNR absolutely free and give Linsipre users the 'choice' to use it? Choice is after all the thouchstone of Linux, is it not? Make no mistake, this fine application would **not** be bundled in the core of Linspire and would not be affiliated to Linspire Inc. in any way.  Users would be free to choose to continue using CNR and paying or... use this new, finely built 3rd party application to have all of the things they want for free with the same  comfort and ease of CNR. 

This is a great idea, isn't it? Then Linspire customers will have the same choice we here in Ubuntu enjoy and love. Shouldn't there be a poll started on the Linspire forums to gauge their interest? Even better if we can get Mark Shuttleworth to explain it and back it up! Yes this would be an excellent idea right? Then all Linspire customers will be made aware of this alternative to CNR and have a choice to make. I'm willing to bet there'll be quite a bit of support (hey if pitched correctly and carefully explained maybe even up to 90% approval) over there for a 3rd party application like this.


Ok, now do you see the problem? Is this something Kevin Carmony would be happy with? Different Linux projects have different principles, commitments, goals and destinations. The ideals of the creator and developers of Ubuntu Linux (Ubuntu yeah? **Ubuntu**, not RMS) are clearly stated on the front page of the project. I don't see these commitments on the front page of Linspire Inc. What I *do* see on the website of  Linspire - "The World's Easiest Desktop Linux!" is a site that looks much like many other corporate websites. I (*literally*) see Walmart, Kmart, Sears etc. 

Fine, Linspire is Linspire and Ubuntu is Ubuntu. *There* is your choice. A 3rd party application such as the one proposed here for Ubuntu would only serve to undermine the chosen ideals and vision of the creator and developers of this distro in the same way that posting in the Linspire forums about a 3rd party app to covert CNR to an absoultely free service would undercut theirs and it's not appreciated by this particular Ubuntu denizen.[/QUOTE]

I totally agree with that and am I 100% behind you. Many people have different visions for Linux and each distro provides thes ideals to us. 

I still have difficulties seeing a humanitarian Linux project with long a term vision encouraging an opportunistic distro with short term monetary goals. At least if the code was open, we could say they are making money with the ethics of open software in mind but it's not even that. When most linux users will be addicted to CNR, it will be Micorsoft all over again since nobody will have another alternative. You'll hear everybody say "yeah you have to pay here and there for everything but it's not like linux is about freedom right?".

### Post by JeffS on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=Kevin Carmony]1. Ease of use.

You may be technical enough to appreciate apt, but most people have no interest in spending time learning new tricks.  The easier things are to use, the less time they have to spend fussing about.

2. Legal use.

Some people don't want to break laws or find it unethical and wrong to steal IP from others.  Maybe they own an iPod, or they want to view movie trailers on the web in Windows Media.  etc.

If you're happy with things as they are, great!  But 98% of the rest of the world would just scratch their head, look at you funny, and turn their Windows computer on.

Kevin[/QUOTE]

Amen to that.  Many in the Linux community either don't understand that, or are too idealistic about "free" software to understand that.

As for me, I'm a big free software proponent, and I'm a big admirer of Richard Stallman, and I actually agree with a lot of his ideas.  But I'm also a pragmatist, and see how most computer users want things to be easy, automatic, legal, and have things "just work".   And they are more than willing to pay the Microsoft tax to get it, even though they are usually paying way too much, because Microsoft gives them those things they want.

Linspire understands this phenomenon.  However, as is evident on this thread, many in the Ubuntu community do not understand the practical needs of many non-technical computer users.

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=aysiu]How exactly does Linspire have short-term monetary goals? Kevin Carmony came on here and said explicitly there was very little profit for Linspire. You can call him a liar, if you want, but I'll call you delusional, then.[/quote]

I'm not sure what happened with the internals of Linspire over the last few years, but from what i've read, in corporate terms, Kevin Carmony is a very recent CEO.

Linspire is not making money. As i've said before, at this time Linux is simply not generally popular enough to make proprietary software economically viable, particularly as Linspire is targetting the "desktop market". Red Hat are doing well with their "corporate customers" but please dont assume that translates to the automatic profitability of a desktop platform.

Therefore, Linspire are forced to skew their ethics slightly in an attempt to remain profitable. Examples? Selling Open Source software like OO and FF as a retail product. (I cant believe that anyone *actually* swallowed their justification for that) Another example? CNR on Ubuntu. Think about it, its brilliant! Ubuntu is by far the most popular Linux Distro, and the majority of Ubuntu users glance at these forums "once in a while". So Kevin's here performing market research, and if he even gets 50% of us to purchase CNR, then Linspire will do quite well...

> CNR itself may be proprietary, but Linspire gives back to the open source community

I give to charity, but 99% of my income stays in my pocket. Linspire are a company, and not an overly profitable one, (thats why Kevin Carmoney keeps coming up with "ingenious" ways to make money). I'm sure for their own moral compass they "give back" to the open source community, but we're not talking about a lot here, just enough to say they've "done it"

> Linspire is neither wholly good nor wholly evil. Think "complex."

Think "desperate".

I hope my post hasnt come accross as a Linspire rant, because that is not what I intended.

I feel sorry for them in a way, as from my point of view, they are in a no-win situation. If Linux was more popular with the masses, then Linspire would be a big company! But its hard to sell, what some people are already rejecting for FREE.

I'm sure Kevin Carmoney is a very decent and hardworking guy, but I dont know what it is, but to me, he comes accross as a "used car salesman". Maybe because he's trying too hard? I dont know.

All I know is, with the market the way it is, I dont envy him or his position at the moment, although I do wish him and his company well (Linspire does have employees after all, and those employees have families)

As far as this issue goes, i'm an idealist, so I hope that it doesnt happen! Although i'm guessing that doesnt make sense? Yeah, i'm pretty much clouded by my ideals on this one! :oops:

### Post by Pandora on 2006-03-04
I like this idea. I know the forums have approached this idea on several different angles. I believe it will benefit ubuntu in the long run. The only way I would be against this deal is if a double standard would be held against another competing(including competition thats free) company in the future and they were denied the same access to ubuntu.

In real life you pay for convenience. You pay for the small bottles of coke in the fridge at the check out counter of the grocery store. They cost more than going to buy the warm coke in bulk and chill it yourself. There are other examples but this is the first one that came to my mind.

With CNR your paying for a similar convenience. Its the convenience of not having to learn much to install software. 

Paying for convenience is a lucrative market when it comes to computers. Microsoft has pretty much been the master of harnessing this. Infact Microsoft has been so good at this that its become unethical in making decisions in order to keep competition out of its business. A lot of us have fled from the software giant for this very reason.

Carmony coming to the ubuntu forums and talking to us users like he has should go to show you all what kind of market place we have on our hands here. Its become valuable enough for entrepreneurs like Carmony to want to move his business assets to our platform to sell.

I find it kind of a shame that Carmony is the only entrepreneur wanting to sell his convenience software to us. Where is his competition, Linux is by no means an anti-competitive operating system. We should be getting offers from competitive services. Maybe its too early in the game to be looking for more people to be trying to take advantage of the free market Ubuntu linux offers in the way linspire is trying to. 

I think CNR and Linspire moving in is a sign of good things to come, because  other competition may someday follow suit. Hopefully when that day comes Mark Shuttleworth and Ubuntu will make the same decisions he makes with linspire and CNR. 

With price mechanics in play "non-free" software will only get better. Mark Shuttleworth and Ubuntu could really reap the benefits of keeping choice in this platform.

---

### Post by matthinckley on 2006-03-04
I still don't understand why the fact that a *client* for a subscription service is closed source is a problem.. it is not limiting anyones freedoms in any way, you still have a choice between CNR and apt / synaptic.  In no way is it limiting anyones choice to have open or closed software on their computer, in fact, from what I have read here, it seems to me most people that only believe in open source don't believe in paying for software or services either, so what difference would it make to them since they wouldn't be signing up for this service in the first place?  If you think that a mere closed source client that is an *alternative, easier to some,* way to download and install open source and proprietary programs goes against the Ubuntu philosophy, you have the freedom to choose not to install it. 

I personally don't think that anyone involved in creating Ubuntu would tell me that they wish I wouldn't install any closed-source apps on my computer.  Doing so would just lessen the target market for Ubuntu, and I don't believe that was Marks goal.  I do understand the open-source principals that you all speak of, but being that this service is optional, offers clear advantages over the open-source alternatives for new, uneducated or unwilling users, and it is a subscription service, I don't think it goes against anything Ubuntu stands for.

Also I don't see much difference in this from Transgamings Cedega.. sure I could spend all day on google finding out how to get my windows games to run under linux myself (just as someone could learn how to compile programs or use native linux game installers) or I could pay Transgaming a subscription to have access to their program that does all this for me (just as paying Linspire for access to their CNR warehouse that lets me click once for programs and games that have already been compiled for Ubuntu and may not be available with apt / synaptic such as legal codecs and proprietary games that you have to pay for). 

I understand that some may feel if CNR was available for Ubuntu it may turn into a Windows/Apple like regime, but Linux is all about choices, and all Linspire is trying to do is offer Ubuntu users another choice.  Mark would never allow CNR to be required, why would he do that?  Linspire doesn't even require its users to pay for the CNR service.  Linspire users have the same choices we do.  They can use apt-get instead if they like.  

I don't understand why someone would deem it wrong for Linspire to make this service available as an option to Ubuntu users.

---

### Post by K.Mandla on 2006-03-04
I have mixed feelings about the idea, partly based on my own inexperience with CNR.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=mstlyevil]If those of us who support choice had that attitude we would not be using Ubuntu because it includes no proprietary software upon a default install. We understand we have the choice of adding it and making Ubuntu our own. The totally free software people have the choice of leaving it all out making Ubuntu conform to their desires. CNR would not change this one bit but yet they act like this would be the end of the world.[/QUOTE]

I agree.  =)  

One's fight for freedom shouldn't come at the expense of another's right to choose.

**Option A) - CNR is not made available:**
Freedom to choose CNR = NO
Freedom to not choose CNR = YES

**Option B) - CNR is made available for those who wish to download and install it:**
Freedom to choose CNR = YES
Freedom to not choose CNR = YES

People complain about monopolies like Microsoft limiting choice, and yet, it would appear that is what some wish to do with Ubuntu.  In the immortal words of Pete Townsend, *"Meet the new boss, same as the old boss."*

Those saying they don't object to a CNR offering, are not even saying THEY would use it, but just that Ubuntu shouldn't impede choice.  

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=aysiu]And, honestly, it doesn't matter how many people are outraged--there's nothing to stop Linspire from porting CNR to Ubuntu. Whether Ubuntu users want to take advantage of their port or not is **their choice**. Freedom Nazis can't stop people from using proprietary software on Ubuntu any more than Microsoft can stop people from using open source applications on Windows.[/QUOTE]

Correct, there would be nothing stopping us from offering it, but we wouldn't.  We would only offer it IF:

- The vast majority of Ubuntu users are OK with this option existing, even if they wouldn't use it themselves.
- Enough Ubuntu users would actually use it, to make it worth the bother and expense on our end.  We have limited resources, so we only want to build things that Linux users can and will use, and that can help spread Linux and open source software to a broader audience.
- Mark is OK with it.  If he were to come to think that this would just upset the "Freedom Fighters" too much, we'd respect that and not offer it.  (In which case, Score:  "Freedom Fighters:" 1  - v - Freedom: 0)

Kevin

### Post by LuisC-SM on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=matthinckley]I still don't understand why the fact that a *client* for a subscription service is closed source is a problem.. it is not limiting anyones freedoms in any way, you still have a choice between CNR and apt / synaptic.  In no way is it limiting anyones choice to have open or closed software on their computer, in fact, from what I have read here, it seems to me most people that only believe in open source don't believe in paying for software or services either, so what difference would it make to them since they wouldn't be signing up for this service in the first place?  If you think that a mere closed source client that is an *alternative, easier to some,* way to download and install open source and proprietary programs goes against the Ubuntu philosophy, you have the freedom to choose not to install it. 

I personally don't think that anyone involved in creating Ubuntu would tell me that they wish I wouldn't install any closed-source apps on my computer.  Doing so would just lessen the target market for Ubuntu, and I don't believe that was Marks goal.  I do understand the open-source principals that you all speak of, but being that this service is optional, offers clear advantages over the open-source alternatives for new, uneducated or unwilling users, and it is a subscription service, I don't think it goes against anything Ubuntu stands for.

Also I don't see much difference in this from Transgamings Cedega.. sure I could spend all day on google finding out how to get my windows games to run under linux myself (just as someone could learn how to compile programs or use native linux game installers) or I could pay Transgaming a subscription to have access to their program that does all this for me (just as paying Linspire for access to their CNR warehouse that lets me click once for programs and games that have already been compiled for Ubuntu and may not be available with apt / synaptic such as legal codecs and proprietary games that you have to pay for). 

I understand that some may feel if CNR was available for Ubuntu it may turn into a Windows/Apple like regime, but Linux is all about choices, and all Linspire is trying to do is offer Ubuntu users another choice.  Mark would never allow CNR to be required, why would he do that?  Linspire doesn't even require its users to pay for the CNR service.  Linspire users have the same choices we do.  They can use apt-get instead if they like.  

I don't understand why someone would deem it wrong for Linspire to make this service available as an option to Ubuntu users.[/QUOTE]
It couldn't be better explained!!!!

What a bright and openminded person you are.

### Post by tageiru on 2006-03-04

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=*John*]That particular code is available freely EVERYWHERE.

If I were to count the number of people who had posted "Downloading Now LOL! \\:D/ " after learning of the code on just **one** of the blogs i've seen it in, the number would be in excess of 300 easily.

300 people who DID NOT pay $50 for the Digital Download? You dont need to be an economist to understand what that means.....

So in the interest of fairness and common decency, I thought it only fair to warn him. Whats wrong with that?

John[/QUOTE]

We have "given away" hundreds of thousands of "free" copies of Linspire via coupon codes, CD distribution in magazines, etc.  We don't worry too much if someone figures out how to get their hands on Linspire.  (Heck, I even posted in this very popular thread that anyone can email me and I'll get them a free copy, which some of you have done.) 

We feel this way for two reasons:

- Our main model is OEM distribution, not our digital download, retail boxes, etc.  Honestly, I don't even look at those sales figures ever.  The only distribution I care about are pre-installed OEM numbers.

- A lot of people who get their hands on Linspire end up trying the 15-day free CNR trial and find out they love it and subscribe.  =)

Some may ask, "So why not just give Linspire away free all the time, like Ubuntu does?"  Because we DO have licensing fees in ours, so the $50 sales help us subsidize the freebies.  If someone is hell bent on NOT paying for Linspire, they can find a way to get their hands on it.  But, if someone doesn't mind paying, and wants to be supportive of the work we're doing, we appreciate their purchase. 

Our main goal with Linspire is to get it pre-installed on zillions of PC's, and the way we make money is via our CNR and other services.  Because enough people pay for our products and services, we are able to have a nice staff of full-time engineers contributing to open source projects, as well as [sponsor a lot of projects directly](http://linspire.com/opensource) (Nvu, Lsongs, Lphoto, Reiser, Moz/FF, etc.).

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=teaker1s]there should be a welcome screen explaining synaptic and CNR  as long as synaptic is installed and CNR has a link to install it if wanted and not preinstalled and pop up balloons appearing all the time then why not- as long as my ubuntu doesn't end up as a marketing platform with pop up ballons and other associated junk.[/QUOTE]

There will always be things Ubuntu users will always find abhorrent, and why there will always be a need for a distro like Linspire.  Even if we did offer a CNR download for Ubuntu, you're right, most may not even know about it, which will always give Linspire the edge over Ubuntu for OEM's and those who need ease of use, out of the box multi media support, nicer fonts, certain drivers, etc.  Linspire has no problem putting things in our distro to make life easy (narrated tutorials, proprietary drivers, codecs, fonts, CNR, etc.) 

To be honest, this is why it might still not make much sense to have a CNR for Ubuntu, because the very people who would need and use it (noobs) are the same people who probably wouldn't know of its existence or how to install it.  The die-hard techies will know all about it, but they are the ones who will not feel they need it.  Catch 22. 

Not only is this a Catch 22 for Linspire as we think about offering CNR for Ubuntu, but it's a Catch 22 for Ubuntu.  Until there are open source replacements that the world is willing to embrace for things like Flash, Java, MP3, Windows Media, DVD, etc., it will be impossible for Ubuntu to live up to their goal of "it just works."  You'll have to change that to "download stuff, break a few copyright laws, do some klickety clack at the command line, and THEN it works."  Again...catch 22.

Kevin

---

### Post by JeffS on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=Kevin Carmony]We have "given away" hundreds of thousands of "free" copies of Linspire via coupon codes, CD distribution in magazines, etc.  We don't worry too much if someone figures out how to get their hands on Linspire.  (Heck, I even posted in this very popular thread that anyone can email me and I'll get them a free copy, which some of you have done.) 

We feel this way for two reasons:

- Our main model is OEM distribution, not our digital download, retail boxes, etc.  Honestly, I don't even look at those sales figures ever.  The only distribution I care about are pre-installed OEM numbers.

- A lot of people who get their hands on Linspire end up trying the 15-day free CNR trial and find out they love it and subscribe.  =)

Some may ask, "So why not just give Linspire away free all the time, like Ubuntu does?"  Because we DO have licensing fees in ours, so the $50 sales help us subsidize the freebies.  If someone is hell bent on NOT paying for Linspire, they can find a way to get their hands on it.  But, if someone doesn't mind paying, and wants to be supportive of the work we're doing, we appreciate their purchase.

Kevin[/QUOTE]


BTW - how is this business plan working out for you?  Are you guys turning a profit yet, or at least breaking even?  The reason I ask is because I believe in that business plan, and I'm rooting for you to succeed. 

 I also believe in the OEM strategy - that's probably the best way for Desktop Linux to succeed.  99% of the "average joe" market is completely uniterested in installing an OS by themselves.  They want what comes with the computer, and to open the box, plug in the computer, and have everything "just work".  

Completely free Linux distros, like Fedora, Debian, and Ubuntu, are great for what they are.  

But commercial Linux distros, like Linspire, Xandros, or Mandriva, who make money by delivering a "value add", have their place too.  And CNR, plus Linspire's very polished desktop, Lsongs, Lphoto, licensed proprietary codecs and formats, all add up to tremendous "value add" on top of free software.  Then add to that the very smart OEM strategy, and you just might have a winner.

Also, my favorite (most used) distro right now is Kanotix, because it's the one giving the most of what I want right now.  However, I use Ubuntu from time to time.  Plus, I have my 72 yr old Mom currently using one of those $200 PCs from Frys with Linspire pre-installed.  She bought it on my recomendation, and I gave her my "computer usage 1A" tutorial, and she was off and running - no fuss, no muss.  For my Mom, a completely non technical user and computer newbie, Linspire is perfect.  Frankly, I would not have her use either Ubuntu or Kanotix - I would have to spend too much time helping her and/or getting her set up - time I can't afford as I'm a married father of 3yr old and 8 month old daughters.

Users of Debian, Fedora, Ubuntu, and other totally free distros, all totally great distros, might have the time to fiddle about with their OS/PCs to get everything working (and probably enjoy doing so, as I often do).  But the above paragraph is a perfect example of the need for the "value add" proposistion that Linspire delivers on so spectacularily.

BTW, Mr Carmony, I would recommend that you make the same proposal to Mepis users.  For one, Mepis is a Debian "hybrid distro" (both Debian testing and unstable packages), which makes it less compatible with Debian repos.  So they have a need for something easy and stable like CNR.  Also, the Mepis users, over at mepislovers.com, seem to be more oriented toward stuff that "just works", rather than totally free software ideals.  Finally, Mepis is very popular as well.  I'm guessing that you might get a little bit better reception over there.

Good luck, and I hope you succeed with both CNR for Ubuntu, and with your overall business plan!

---

I would love to have this for my distros, it's about the only way to make APT/Synaptic better.

Would I rather Ubuntu repositories had that? Yep. and the pretty pictures/presentation of software packages for the Windows folks I move to GNU/Linux, I wish the Ubuntu repositories had that as well, but they don't, yet. Don't get me started on driver instalation (ATI you know who you are).

I also fear it might still be important that we (the whole world) get there sooner rather than later. We are in a race, people say users will reject DRM, I say users won't give up their content. I don't think our freedom is won yet, and if if i have to choose between  having folks on Ubuntu with CNR or Vista with Firefox & OO guess which I pick? I'll fight the CNR battle tomorrow, and there is *zero* comparison between Microsoft and Linspire, that is a braindead statement.

Needless to say I hope Linspire does "port" CNR to Ubuntu. I hope Ubuntu keeps pushing the state of the art in software delivery. I also hope that we could come up with some more creative responses to this opportunity.

rant off, thanks for your time.

### Post by mstlyevil on 2006-03-04
Kevin

  To test the waters you could develop a very simplified CNR for Ubuntu that just offers basic things like the codecs, DVD player and other basic software on a pay as needed basis. If this does well then you will know there is a market for the full CNR package and then can spend the extra money to devlop it. If it fails, then you will not have spent no where near the money and the loss will not be as great. The other thing you could do is create a web page for Ubuntu users to  sign up on to express their wish for a product like CNR. By signing up on this wish page you will be saying yes I will buy it and use it. You can get a much more accurate guage for the demand for this by hard numbers. If the numbers are high enough to genrate a profit then you can start development. If not then nothing was really lost and at least both Linspire and Ubuntu are both better for it.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=pkarlos_76]He never said he was selling the software, he's offering a support service for the software, you can't expect to get support for free software for free. He is selling a service that makes setting up and configuring the software easier by doing it for you. Do you goto IKEA and and expect them to build it for you for free, because you bought a package that must be assembled first?[/QUOTE] He may not have made that 100% clear in this thread, but Linspire's own website is quite clear about what you're buying with CNR. Here's an excerpt (my emphasis added): > **What is CNR?**
CNR stands for "click and run". It is a _software delivery service_ designed for Linspire users that makes it easy to install Linux software.

With the CNR Service you can install more than 2,000 _FREE Linux software titles_ direct from the CNR Warehouse - all with just a single click.

In fact, the CNR Service is the easiest _way to install Linux software_. Simply click the software you want and it installs on your computer and is ready to run.

And the CNR Service gives you more than just _one-click access to tons of free software._

You also get a powerful, easy way to manage your entire software library. Like customizable aisles where you can install entire groups of software with a single click. Perfect for setting up a new computer!

### Post by Grey on 2006-03-04
This thread is 70 pages long... and I honestly don't feel up to reading that.  But here's my view on it.

Synaptic already does a lot of what I gather CNR does.  However, Synaptic has one glaring flaw.  It doesn't have any non-free or commercial software, which some users would like to have.  CNR does.  For that reason, I would support CNR coming to Ubuntu.

However, I might say that a better idea would be to implement a service such as Steam on Windows, where participating developers could publish their commercial software.  This is not the same as CNR, as it would be strictly for services that are commercial, and there would be no fee for using the service... just a fee for purchasing the software.  You develop a game that you want to sell to Linux users (Doom 3 perhaps?)?  Then publish it on Ubuntu's version of Steam.  I can see a few early adopters of the services being something like this:

-Crossover Office
-Uplink
-Darwinia
-Cedega

Of which, I would probably buy Uplink.  Or maybe Cedega.  At any rate, that's the sort of service I would use.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-04
[quote=Grey]Synaptic already does a lot of what I gather CNR does. However, Synaptic has one glaring flaw. It doesn't have any non-free or commercial software, which some users would like to have. CNR does. For that reason, I would support CNR coming to Ubuntu.[/quote] Synaptic has plenty of non-free software if you enable Universe and Multiverse repositories. I installed non-free Flash and MP3 codecs using Synaptic.

[QUOTE=freedomforme]
I wonder if all this is even possible. How do we know the codecs are legal, in particular the dvd codecs? Legal is usually decided in court, so has this been?[/QUOTE] This is from Linspire's website: > The Linspire DVD player is a software multimedia player that includes legal, licensed commercial-quality codecs and auto-detection of DVDs to enhance the DVD playback experience under Linspire 4.5 and higher.

Using the DVD drive in your computer, the Linspire DVD player plays back DVD movies, as well as audio CDs and VCDs. Linspire DVD player also plays multimedia files like AVI, MOV, WMV, and MP3 from local disk drives, and displays multimedia streamed over the Internet.

A full list of supported formats supported and links to other DVD-related information can be found here.


How is the Linspire DVD player different from Xine and other DVD players available for Linux?
The Linspire DVD player is actually based on the Xine player, but there are three main differences: First, the Linspire DVD player includes a commercial license for the DVD playback decoding so you don't have to find, buy and install this on your own (this can be expensive and a tricky, complicated process).

Second, Linspire DVD player has been optimized for ease-of-use, making it one-click easy to install and use.

And third, Linspire DVD player has been integrated fully with Linspire, making things like auto-play possible so that when you put a DVD into your Linspire computer, it recognizes it and loads the DVD software automatically. Linspire users can also, if they choose, install the regular Xine player (without the items mentioned above) at no charge via CNR.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-04
[QUOTE=freedomforme]
But if I purchase, download and install from their respective websites then I get a license and can use it on other OSs

Also Limewire pro in CNR is $17.95, from Limewire it is only $18.88 I think. And I think win4lin is about a 10 dollar difference...[/QUOTE] CNR isn't for dollar savings--it's *obviously* not for dollar savings, as you have to pay a subscription to get *free* software installed easily. CNR is for convenience. If you pay Limewire $18.88 and then download it, how are you going to install it? Look around the Wiki? Ask questions on the forums?

I can't tell you how many times I've seen people asking how to install Cedega or Limewire.

What I'm saying is that some people may want to pay for the convenience of having things installed with **one click**. Synaptic is pretty much the same thing, but it can't install Limewire or Cedega or Crossover Office.

---

### Post by Brynster on 2006-03-05
Ok fellow ubuntu users.

I am an ex Linspire user. It appears that KC is trying to promote a service that 

1. Employs a rudimentary form of digital rights management on the packages you down loa. Thats right even the ones that are freely distributatble under different versions of the various GPL/GNU licences.  Now before anybody pipes up and shouts me down about proctecting copyright. I have no issue with the commercial stuff being protected in some way. HOWEVER the free as in licence unrestricted and as in beer stuf SHOULD NOT BE PROTECTED. as we have the right granted to us in the licence to modify and or change parts of the code as long as we feed it back or publish the scource code.


2 Using CnR may break you system.

Let me explain a little further. The version of product "a" requires dependcy "x" to work, if however you version "y" or "z" of the dependcy then when you install product "a" from CnR it will install all its dependent packages as well. This will mean you have version "x" and your version "y" for example. This causes a conflict as both dependents will try to run when they are called. creating instability, crashes, and worst case scenerio forcing you to run a fresh install.

Just thought some facts should be explained to make things a little clearer to you all. I am an ex Linspire user and CnR does work very well. Its just IMO not suitable for Ubuntu. Whose main philosophy is freedom......

---

### Post by darkmatter on 2006-03-05
No... dependancy is not freedom... and Linspire's 'hand-holding' in that way... though understandable for its target audience.... the average joe who wouldn't know squat about fixing something if he busted it by updating himself...  (as, lets face it... most users blame the os/company for their problems... which affects the userbase for said os... because the *user* is always right-- and tends to blame the manufacturer... sadly), and Linspire is infamously known for non-Linspire packages breaking the system... does create that dependancy.  Though I can understand why their native system is such... and do not see it as *shady*. Remember: like Novell and others... Linspire is a commercial distro... including support. Now... I know I would rather support bugs with my product than 'user-implemented' bugs that someone brought to the system themselves.... I would still support both... but would attempt to find a way to reduce the percentage of the later..

If it does become an option... this is how I see the implementation going in Ubuntu... because I doubt Mark would have it any other way.

1) Ubuntu CNR would not interfere with non-CNR software installations (Synaptic/manual), as uby is about-- along with many other things-- freedom of choice

2) Ubuntu would regulate content control for a) binary compatibility b) to ensure that the same package versions were available to both CNR and non-CNR customers

Anything else... and it wouldn't be Ubuntu

From the Linspire site:

> Source Code

The Linspire operating system is a combination of open source code and proprietary software. The open source software programs included in Linspire are licensed (or sublicensed) to the user under the GNU General Public License ("GPL") and other similar open source license agreements (collectively, "OSLAs") which, among other rights, permit the user to copy, modify and redistribute certain programs, or portions thereof, and have access to the source code.

In compliance with the applicable OSLAs, the source code for the open source portions of the Linspire operating system are available for download here.

Open source source code for individual products found in the Warehouse can be found by clicking the Specifications link on the page for the product (example).

Alternatively, you may obtain CD(s) with the source code of the open source programs we distribute by emailing your name and address along with the names of the products for which you are seeking source code to [email]sourcecode@linspireinc.com[/email]. As a condition to sending you the CD(s), we may charge you our costs for providing you with the CD(s).

Proof enough??? ;)

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-05
From the [MPAA's website](http://www.mpaa.org/DVD_FAQ.asp): > The Linux argument is a false issue. It has always been in the interest of the Motion Picture industry that there be as many legitimately licensed DVD players as possible, including those using non-Windows operating systems. However the argument that DeCSS was written for Linux players is simply false. The De-CSS utility was written for Windows-based software, not Linux.

Also, the development of two, separate, licensed DVD players for Linux systems - which use the CSS system - were recently announced. Sigma Designs ([www.sigmadesigns.com](www.sigmadesigns.com)) and InterVideo Inc. ([www.intervideo.com](www.intervideo.com)) both announced the roll-out of LICENSED, LEGAL Linux-based DVD players.

[QUOTE=freedomforme]
WinDVD was going to offer LinDVD, said there was a demand for it but then it disappeared...what happened there? ### Post by LuisC-SM on 2006-03-05
[QUOTE=Brynster]Ok fellow ubuntu users.

I am an ex Linspire user. It appears that KC is trying to promote a service that 

1. Employs a rudimentary form of digital rights management on the packages you down loa. Thats right even the ones that are freely distributatble under different versions of the various GPL/GNU licences.  Now before anybody pipes up and shouts me down about proctecting copyright. I have no issue with the commercial stuff being protected in some way. HOWEVER the free as in licence unrestricted and as in beer stuf SHOULD NOT BE PROTECTED. as we have the right granted to us in the licence to modify and or change parts of the code as long as we feed it back or publish the scource code.


2 Using CnR may break you system.

Let me explain a little further. The version of product "a" requires dependcy "x" to work, if however you version "y" or "z" of the dependcy then when you install product "a" from CnR it will install all its dependent packages as well. This will mean you have version "x" and your version "y" for example. This causes a conflict as both dependents will try to run when they are called. creating instability, crashes, and worst case scenerio forcing you to run a fresh install.

Just thought some facts should be explained to make things a little clearer to you all. I am an ex Linspire user and CnR does work very well. Its just IMO not suitable for Ubuntu. Whose main philosophy is freedom......[/QUOTE]
Regarding your Point Nr 1.

I respect it but no necesarily agree in all points

Regarding Point Nr. 2.
Please, be so kind to post your Linspire and CnR versions, 'cause AFAIK there were several HARDWARE issues (even at present) with Linspire and CnR. as with most of distributions, but regarding your statement I beleive is completely unacurate.

I'm a Ubuntu Breezy 5.10 and Linspire 5.0.348 user and none of the above reflects the exact truth.

AFAICR, there was an issue with cinelerra causing some of the comments on your statement, and (even when I do not know the reason) cinelerra is not in the CnR's warehouse any more. 

I'm very happy with Ubuntu 'cause I preffer the APT-GET way than CnR, but my children and wife do not complain using Linspire at all.

-Edit- 
In this LapTop I used to have Ubuntu BB 5.10 and Ubuntu Dapper Testing, but I had so many issues with codecs that I preffered to Install Linspire.

Anyways, I love Ubuntu as much as I love Linspire, (so does my family). In my personall desktop I have Ubuntu BB 5.10 and it's being used by my 8 year old child, and Believe me... CnR could not be the best service but if it ever happens to be (AS AN OPTION) in Ubuntu, I will be the happiest man.

Kind Regards.

### Post by gardion on 2006-03-05
A few points I'd like to make. 

1.  I'm ok with CNR being a third party proprietery app that is not installed by default on Ubuntu.  I don't think we have a right to restrict choice although we can encourage people to use freer options by having High quality free alternatives included with ubuntu on the desktop.  (I'm really hopeful about Gnash and gcj).  This will give people less reason to use non-free alternatives and encourage more use of free software.

2.  Honestly I think what most people would like CNR for would be to give them access to  proprietery software that costs money or legal non-free codecs.  While we could individually ask companies to support this it seems Linspire has done this work for us so we may as well use it for now.  Also if Ubuntu went and did this it would very much look more like a conflict of interest in encouraging free software.   I hope if we encourage linspire to do offer CNR than other companies may also offer stuff to Ubbunt as well.

3.  I think it's a little funny to use a proprietery app (CNR client) to install some free software, since there is really no reason that Ubuntu couldn't do that themselves.  (I do know there are advantages to use CNR but that doesn't change things).  However, I don't have a problem of using a proprietery application to install proprietery softwarers and legal non-free codecs.

Having said this if CNR does offer free apps that are also in Ubuntu I wouldn't object but I think it wouldn't be valuable.  I think the free and proprietery stuff should be in separate repositories / warehouses so that you could use CNR for only installing proprietery software if you want and use apt-get to install free software

Basically I'm hopping if I use CNR I can use it to only install things that are non-free and not available in other ubuntu repositories.

1. Impi Linux is NOT Ubuntu. It's Ubuntu-based. Mark owns share in Impi, but not Impi itself. It has options not available in Ubuntu.

2. DVD playback is because Impi has licensed the codecs. Ubuntu will not license the codecs for Ubuntu because then that would mean Ubuntu would no longer be free. This ties in quite nicely with 1, by the way.

3. Google does not currently have plans to release "Goobuntu" as I've heard it referred to.

### Post by Bandit on 2006-03-05
[QUOTE=Bragador]A quicker but riskier path but I respect that. It must come from your business side :mrgreen:[/QUOTE]
I tell ya. I been using linux for a long time. 8 years this summer. And to me gnu/linux has always had the potential to be a great operating system. Most of the times in looks and stability it supases windows, only to be riveld by OSX. Am I very passsionate about gnu/linux. No its software. What I am passionate about is seeing many freinds and family being screwed over by companies such as microsoft and being miss lead to belive windows its the best. I bad part is that now they do belive M$ and such is the best and bugs & stability issues are common place. 
I believe and from experience gnu/linux offers more and at a more economical price, even some times free linux Ubuntu.
I like many others are constantly faced with users complaining Ubuntu linux is to hard.. Lucky for me I never tried to get anyone else to use Slackware way back in the day.
I see opertunities like CNR as a way to help make Ubuntu and other linux distros easier. Would I much rather see a opensource version out on the market at much lower competitive prices. O' yea.. But they are not out yet. CNR bascily has the market cornered. I still belive that we can use them and still achive our goals to wide spread the gnu/linux OS. Linspire has did wonders in the linux market. I wouldnt run Linspire as I think its kinda silly to pay for a product then repay for updates and software. I think they should be included in the product price. I totally understand the codec and non GPL software side and thats the one big reason I am down for CNR being offered for Ubuntu.
Like I siad before, I think CNR is nessary to help gain market share.
After we have gained enough market share that Linux is a common name is households just like Windows and Mac then we can try to swade everyone to the more oensource side of everything.
Cheers,
Joey
